vii 
 
Executive Summary 
 
The importance of native plant materials is increasing as disturbance events, such as 
wildfire, drought, and livestock grazing continue throughout the western United States.  These 
disturbance events often create environments suitable for the invasion and establishment of non-
native species further threatening native ecosystems. The need for seeding these disturbed lands 
has created a large demand for locally-adapted native plants and seed.  Use of locally-adapted 
plant materials has been shown to improve the success of restoration projects and maintain 
genetic diversity, which is a vital mechanism by which native plant communities can adapt and 
evolve.  Due to the lack of availability and costs for these materials however, many public 
policies continue to allow the use of non-native plant materials.  The use of these materials can 
compromise the success of restoration projects and can lead to further ecosystem degradation.  
With an increasing number of large-scale restoration/rehabilitation projects within northern 
Arizona, agencies and organizations are interested in ways to develop an increased supply of 
locally-adapted plant materials. 
This study assesses the opportunity to develop a native plant and seed industry in 
northern Arizona.  To do this, supply and demand surveys were created and administered to 
identify the most salient issues needed to be considered when initiating a native plant materials 
market within the region.  Through an extensive interview and searching process, participants for 
each of the surveys were identified.  Survey questions were developed based on preliminary 
information gained through interviews.  Survey topics included native plant materials purchasing 
and expenditures, native plant sales, use and needs, issues and concerns, equipment and 
operations, collaboration and funding, and policies and regulations.  Survey pre-testing allowed 
for testing of survey question comprehension, efficiency relevancy, and format organization.   

viii 
 
 
The survey revealed several important issues to both suppliers and buyers of native seed.  
Both such entities agree that locally-adapted plant materials are important, but that extreme 
elevation and climate conditions within the region make defining the term “local genotype” 
difficult.  From a supplier’s perspective, inconsistent demand and insufficient knowledge on how 
to successfully produce native plant materials were important limiting factors. Yet, purchasers 
appear to be discouraged by the lack of availability and cost of these materials.  It appears that 
within the current market, there is an inconsistent demand for native plant materials due to 
unpredictability of natural disturbance events such as fire size and frequency or the extent and 
severity of drought.  In addition, organization/agency policy often drives decisions on whether 
native plant materials are purchased, and, while most agencies have policies requiring the use of 
native seeds, lack of their availability allows for the continued use of non-native species. An 
inconsistent and unreliable demand has made suppliers hesitant to further the development of 
native plant materials. Stewardship contracting (where federal agencies contract with 
communities in order to help provide a continuing source of local income and employment while 
restoring and maintaining healthy forest ecosystems), or buy-back programs in which agencies 
contract with growers to buy-back a portion of the materials produced,  could be used to provide 
suppliers the guaranteed demand they necessitate.     
Although an abundance of information is available regarding native plant harvesting, 
cleaning, propagation, and proper storage, this information is not adequately disseminated.  
Increased communication, information sharing, and partnerships among producers, government 
agencies, land managers and organizations proves to be important for initiation of a successful 
market.  A regional working group, the Northern Arizona Native Seed Alliance (NANSA) has 
begun working toward increasing information sharing and collaboration on native plant projects 

ix 
 
among its members.  Members include individuals, government agencies, non-profit 
organizations, and businesses from the region.  However, NANSA is still in its infancy. In  order 
for its efforts to become more effective, a stronger involvement among its members needs to 
occur. 
The largest factors in determining native plant materials supply and demand appears to be 
the needs of government agencies involved in landcape-scale revegetation and their associated 
native plant and seed certification policies.  Agencies, particularly federal and state, will continue 
to play an important role in market development.   In using two successful restoration programs 
within the Great Basin and the Uncompahgre Plateau as native plant market models, most 
funding for these programs comes from federal agencies.    The amount of funding needed for 
initiation of a native plant materials project in northern Arizona is estimated to be between 
$500,000 to $800,000 for the first year and between $2,000,000 and $6,000,000 over a five year 
period.  Investigation of ways to secure this amount of funding should be a top priority of 
regional land managers involved in a regional native plant market.

1 
 
I.  Project Overview 
 
Disturbance events, such as wildfire, drought, and livestock grazing continue to rise 
throughout the western United States threatening native plant communities.  Currently, the 
supply of native plant materials is limited throughout northern Arizona and elsewhere in the 
Southwest.  Due to an increasing need for locally-adapted plant materials (Richards et al., 1998; 
USDI and USDA, 2002; Shaw et al., 2005; Smith et al., 2007), many federal, state, private and 
nonprofit entities have become interested in the possibility of producing native plant materials.  
This study was developed to obtain baseline information and reveal the most salient issues 
concerning the development of a localized (northern Arizona) native plant materials market.  
With the information and insight gained from this study, useful recommendations on how to 
proceed with initiation of the market are made.  Further, this study can be used as a model for 
native plant materials development outside northern Arizona.  
 
Problem Statement 
The need for native plant materials is increasing as large-scale disturbance events, such as 
wildfires, drought, livestock grazing, and further disruption from invasive species which results 
from these disturbances continue throughout the western United States.  Current federal policy 
stresses the need for seeding these disturbed lands (USDI and USDA, 2002; USDI and USDA, 
2006) and thus has created a large demand for locally-adapted native plants and seed.  However, 
a minimal local supply of these plant materials has resulted in federal, state, tribal, and private 
dollars being spent on native plant materials from non-regional and sometimes out-of-state 
sources.  Using genotypes from distant sources can not only compromise seeding success, but 

2 
 
can lead to ecological problems such as poor adaptation and genetic contamination over time 
(Rogers and Montalvo, 2004).   
As the extent of disturbance events increases in the Southwest, revegetation of disturbed 
lands has become essential.  In recent years, the trend has been towards an increased use of 
native species.  Land managers are now faced with issues concerning the genotypes of native 
species used within the region.  Current research indicates that the use of local genotypes 
considerably improves the success of restoration projects.  Plants grown from locally-adapted 
native seed have been shown to display increased growth, survival, and production when 
compared to non-local seed (Knapp and Rice, 1994; Belnap, 1995; Linhart, 1995; Mortlock, 
2000).  Sound land management practices demand the use of locally-adapted plant materials to 
properly restore native plant communities and associated ecological processes.  Reliable 
quantities of these materials are needed to sufficiently supply the increasing number of large-
scale revegetation projects within northern Arizona.   
Research, applying climate projections using global circulation models (GCMs), 
consistently indicates that increased temperature will likely extend fire seasons throughout the 
western United States, and predicts that fires will become larger and more severe (McKenzie et 
al., 2003; Westerling et al., 2006, Seager et al. 2007).  Extreme fire conditions have negative 
effects on plant reestablishment including increases in exotic species (Schoennagel et al., 2004; 
Freeman et al., 2007; Kerns et al., 2006) and seedling and seed mortality, both of which lead to 
lower species abundance and richness (Schoennagel et al., 2004; Hunter and Omi, 2006).  Under 
these climate predictions, demand for post-fire seeding materials will increase if native plant 
communities are unable to regenerate on their own.  Beyond post-fire restoration, revegetation 
projects are an important part of land management for a variety of federal and state agencies’ 

3 
 
activities associated with invasive species management, livestock grazing, wildlife habitat 
management, roadside rehabilitation, and recreational and agricultural use.   
The development of native plant materials has been slow to emerge.  Past trends have 
focused on research and large-scale production of native tree species and use of low-cost non-
native species.  Government policies have begun to reflect increasing attention on the use of 
natives for seeding activities, but due to the high cost and lack of materials, seeding policies 
continue to allow the use of non-natives (Wolfson and Sieg, in review).  Research on genetics 
and propagation of many native shrubs, forbs and grasses (Monsen et al., 2004), which are of 
interest for current restoration practices, remains limited.  A lack of information needed to 
produce these plant materials on a large-scale basis resulted in only small-scale production.  This 
has kept costs for native seed high which often restricts the amount of seed buyers are able or 
willing to purchase. 
At present, wildland collections from unmanaged natural stands are a major source of 
seed for most species.  However, year to year fluctuations in seed production results in high 
market prices making it difficult to include certain desirable species in restoration and 
revegetation projects (Stevens et al., 1996).   
 
Geographic Scope 
This study focuses on regions within Arizona which are north of the Mogollon Rim 
(Figure 1.1).  The Mogollon Rim forms a long escarpment which stretches southwest of Flagstaff 
and runs southeast following the Coconino-Tonto National Forest boundaries to the White 
Mountains of eastern Arizona along the southern boundary of the Apache-Sitgreaves National 
Forest.  The Mogollon Rim also marks the southern edge of the Colorado Plateau in Arizona.   

4 
 
This study focuses on three main ecological regions above the Mogollon Rim; (1)  
Ponderosa pine (Pinus ponderosa) forest, (2) pinyon pine (Pinus edulis)-juniper (Juniperus 
osteosperma and Juniperus monosperma) woodland, and (3) semi-arid grassland and shrubland.    
 
 
Figure 1.1 Region of focus (redline depicts study area north of the Mogollon Rim) 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 

5 
 
Objectives 
The main objective of this study is to investigate the feasibility of producing native plant 
materials within the northern Arizona region. Other objectives are to: 
1. Identify and assess current and future needs and concerns of the stakeholders involved in 
both the supply and demand of native plant materials.  
2. Investigate current governmental and state policies for the use of native species, native 
plant programs, native plant material harvesting regulations, and native seed certification 
mandates and procedures. 
3. Assess critical components involved in developing a successful commercial native plant 
materials market by examining the structure and operations of seed suppliers currently in 
place. 
4. Develop models or scenarios that may lead to an available native plant materials source. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 

6 
 
II. Introduction 
Differentiation between the terms restoration and rehabilitation is essential when 
discussing the use of native plant materials.  Both of these terms are used throughout this study 
and, therefore, defining them is important to understanding the information this study presents.  
Both restoration and rehabilitation activities are important tools in protecting native plant 
communities structure and function.   Disturbance events, such as climate change, drought, 
wildfire, invasive species, herbivory, urbanization, and the use of non-local plant materials can 
have adverse effects on native plant communities.  As disturbance events increase, the use of 
locally-adapted plant materials during restoration and rehabilitation will be increasingly 
valuable. 
 
Definition of Restoration and Rehabilitation  
According to the Society for Ecological Restoration International (SER), “ecological 
restoration is the process of assisting the recovery of an ecosystem that has been degraded, 
damaged, or destroyed” (SER, 2002) with the ultimate goal of reestablishing indigenous 
ecosystem processes which make it resilient and self-sustaining (Clewell and Aronson, 2007).  
Ecological restoration is a process which may take centuries to achieve.  Long-term commitment 
of land managers is an essential component to its success (Burke and Mitchell, 2007).  In 
comparison, rehabilitation seeks to repair degraded ecosystem processes, restore pre-existing 
conditions, and recreate sustaining ecosystems with the primary goal of increasing ecosystem 
productivity (SER, 2002; Aronson et al., 1993).  Pre-existing conditions and sustainable 
ecosystems in this sense may not necessarily be those which are fully indigenous.  Furthermore, 

7 
 
land managers use rehabilitation practices when restoration may be deemed impossible and 
instead seeks to achieve desired conditions as rapidly as possible (Aronson et al., 1993).   
 
Large-scale disturbances and effects on native vegetation 
Over the past century, changing climate conditions, fire suppression, non-native species 
invasion, exploitation of rangelands, urbanization, and use of non-local genotype plant materials 
have lead to an alteration of soil and plant resources and an overall loss of native plant 
community productivity and diversity (Milton et al., 1994; Monsen and McArthur, 1995; 
Holmgren and Scheffer, 2001).   Native plant communities have in turn become more susceptible 
to pathogens, non-native species, and their long-term sustainability threatened (Schmid, 1994; 
Hufford and Mazer, 2003).  
 
Climate Change 
Climate change models provide strong evidence that increased CO2, temperature, and 
altered precipitation patterns will affect the survival and growth of individuals, the distribution 
and composition of species, and fire relations within communities (Ryan, 1991; Bachelet et al., 
2001).  Both CO2 and temperature are key factors affecting plant development and function 
(Morison and Lawlor, 1999).  Enriched CO2 levels have the ability to alter primary and 
secondary metabolism as a result of increases in photosynthesis and carbohydrate production 
over plant growth, maintenance and storage demands (Farrar and Williams, 1991; Veteli et al, 
2002).  These changes will dictate plant productivity.  Although elevated temperatures can 
increase net primary production (NPP) through enhanced photosynthesis and nutrient availability 
through higher rates of decomposition, excessive temperatures have the ability to decrease net 

8 
 
primary production by decreasing soil moisture, inactivate enzymes and decrease carbohydrate 
pools by increasing plant respiration (Peng and Apps, 1999), and amplify the effects of elevated 
CO2 (Veteli et al., 2002).  As extreme temperatures rapidly decrease soil moisture, whole plant 
communities will undergo water related stress leading to decreased ability to compete with 
nearby species ultimately constraining plant distribution and abundance.  (Wittaker, 1975; 
Niering and Lowe, 1984; Hanson and Wiltzin, 2000).  Overall, community responses to changes 
in temperature, CO2, and precipitation as well as species adaptability will play an important role 
in determining species distributions and ability to succeed under extreme climate change events 
(Aber et al., 2001). 
 
Drought 
Generally, the majority of the western United States is characterized by periodic drought. 
Water stress related issues are not uncommon throughout many western forests.  Concerns 
regarding native vegetation impacts due to drought have become amplified because of increasing 
rates of climate change (Hanson and Wiltzin, 2000).  In the arid regions where drought stress is 
important, a longer growing season will prolong drought stress which may lead to reduced 
growth resulting from altered water use efficiency and water stress conditions (Hanson and 
Wiltzin, 2000; Aber et al., 2001).  Repeated episodes of drought affecting soil water availability 
could constrain seedling establishment and lead to increased drought-induced mortality (Hanson 
and Wiltzin, 2000).   Mortality of drought-intolerant species will alter plant community 
composition, decrease plant diversity, and create conditions favorable to non-native species 
invasions.  
 

9 
 
Wildfire 
Climate change will have a profound affect on fire regimes within the western United 
States by increasing the area burned, fire season duration, and fire frequency and severity, all of 
which could have an immediate and significant impact on ecosystems (Nitschke and Innes, 
2006; Flannigan et al., 2000).  Several studies have shown that drought will induce reductions in 
decomposition rates.  Lower decomposition rates may cause a buildup of organic material on 
the forest floor which has the potential to alter fire regimes (Hanson and Wiltzin, 2000; Dale et 
al., 2001).   
Many native species within western United States ponderosa pine (Pinus ponderosa) 
forests are well adapted to fires of low intensity (Hunter and Omi, 2006) and respond positively 
when burning conditions are optimal.  When in combination, optimal fire frequency and low 
fire intensity have shown to favor native understory species richness and diversity and biomass 
productivity by decreasing competition and litter cover (Knapp and Seastedt, 1986; Brockway 
and Lewis, 1997; Laughlin et al. 2004).   However, research of many crown-fire ecosystems, 
those accustomed to longer fire intervals, supports predictions that species abundance and 
richness may be lower following relatively short fire intervals.  As time between fires shortens, 
fire intolerant species may be unable to recover sufficiently, leading to decreased species 
abundance (Schoennagel et al., 2004; Thanos and Daskalakou, 2000), and more specifically that 
of dominant long-lived trees and shrubs.   
Fire severity also plays an important role in controlling post-fire ecosystem structure and 
function through direct impacts on underground plant root and reproduction tissues and soil seed 
banks (Dale et al., 2001).  Variations in fire severity can occur within a single burned landscape. 
These variations affect regeneration of understory plant communities differentially and have the 

10 
 
potential to influence native plant distribution and abundance (McKenzie et al., 2004), 
reestablishment (Turner and Romme, 1994), and the distance seeds will have to travel for 
regeneration (Flannigan et al., 2000).  Results from a study by conducted by Turner and Romme 
(1994), showed that extensive areas that experienced fires of high severity are likely to have few 
resprouting individuals and much of the seedbank destroyed.  Seed banks and seed supply are 
important sources of recovering vegetation following a disturbance.  As a result, severe fires tend 
to reduce total native plant coverage and species richness (Wang and Kemball, 2005).  
  
Invasive Species 
Available literature suggests that non-native plant invasions not only affect native plant 
diversity but can alter entire ecosystem structure and function.  The success of introduced non-
native species may be attributed to a combination of human-caused introductions, biological 
strategies, environmental characteristics of the invaded ecosystem, and biotic interactions within 
the plant community.  These mechanisms have enabled exotic species to invade and outcompete 
natives by being competitively superior.  For example, non-native grass species were 
traditionally selected and developed to revegetate and improve rangelands based on their ability 
to quickly establish, hardiness, adaptability, and ability to withstand grazing (Monsen and Shaw, 
2001; Rogers and Montalvo, 2004).  Because of exotic species competitive abilities, they were 
able to replace native plant species and create biotic homogeneity that has reduced native species 
diversity (Blair, 2001). 
Disturbance effects on exotic species pose yet another threat to native plant communities.  
Conditions of increased availability of light, nutrients, and bare ground have been associated 
with the establishment of non-native plant species (Schoennagel et al., 2004; Wang and Kemball, 

11 
 
2005; Freeman et al., 2007) due to their ability to outcompete native species by capturing 
resources necessary for growth (Kerns et al., 2006).  For example, as forest fires become more 
frequent, fire-adapted species will play a major role in shaping forest ecosystem composition, 
structure and function.  Shorter fire-return intervals facilitate invasion by non-native annuals, 
many of which are nitrogen fixers.  As nitrogen availability declines after fire, as studies 
throughout many western coniferous forests have shown, nitrogen fixers will have a competitive 
advantage (McKenzie et al., 2004; Schoennagel et al., 2004).  As non-native fire-adapted species 
exploit fire prone environments, selection against many longer lived fire-intolerant species 
normally associated with late successional stages in western forests may occur (Nitschke and 
Innes, 2006).   
 
Domestic and Wildlife Herbivory  
Herbivores, both domestic and wildlife, depend heavily on native plant communities and 
in turn have the potential to affect and cause major changes to plant community composition and 
structure (Augustine and McNaughton, 1998).   Herbivory has the ability to negatively alter 
growth rates, nutrient uptake ability, and litter quality, all of which have subsequent effects on 
entire ecosystem processes (Pastor and Cohen, 1997).  
Livestock grazing density plays a large role in determining overall effects on the plant 
community.   Stabilized levels of livestock grazing have been used to maintain palatable species 
and increase their growth potential (Augustine and McNaughton, 1998).  However, as cattle 
density increases, selectivity on forage species declines due to reduced availability of the 
preferred species, leading to communities dominated by unpalatable species (Healy, 1997).  
Intense grazing affects ecosystem processes by reducing native herbaceous cover and litter, 

12 
 
disturbance and compaction of soils, reduced water infiltration rates, increased soil erosion and 
decrease ability of vegetation recovery (Baker and Guthery, 1990; Fleischner, 1994; Milton et 
al., 1994; Belsky and Blumenthal, 1997; Beschta et al., 2004).  Thus, poor grazing practices can 
decrease native plant diversity (Kimball and Schiffman, 2003; Fleischner, 1994) and increase 
non-native invasions (Beschta et al., 2004).   
 
Urbanization 
As population growth within the western United States continues (Campbell, 1996) rapid 
conversion of natural landscapes into urban landscapes will persist.  Urban environments are 
often associated with increased nitrogen inputs which indirectly affect plants, microbes, and soil 
chemistry.  Within urbanized landscapes, negative effects on ecosystem processes such as 
decomposition, nitrogen-cycling, and plant community structure are attributed to increased 
average temperatures (McDonnell et al., 1997).  Large amounts of disturbance created by 
building roads and structures destroy native vegetation and increases the likelihood of invasion 
and establishment of non-native species (McKinney, 2002; Sukopp, 2004).  Effects of exotic 
invasions as previously mentioned severely affect native plant community structure and function.  
Other disturbances associated with urbanization include soil compaction, mowing, and herbicide 
application, all of which have the potential to negatively affect native plant communities.  
Urbanization is a rapidly growing cause of changes in native species richness and composition 
(Sukopp, 2004).   
    

13 
 
Plant Genotypes 
Knowing a plant’s genetic orgin (genotype) can strongly influence the establishment 
success of revegetation projects (Aubry et al., 2005).  Although land managers try to use as much 
locally-adapted plant materials as feasible, large-scale projects often necessitate the use of plant 
materials from non-local sources because of limited funding and resources (McKay et al., 2005).  
These materials represent genotypes that may be poorly adapted to the restoration site conditions 
(Hufford and Mazer, 2003). To complicate matters, most suppliers produce plant materials which 
originate from few genotypes (Williams, 2001).  Subsequently, restoration projects incorporate 
seed from less genetically diverse individuals (McKay et al., 2005).  Plant populations that are 
less genetically diverse are more susceptible to pathogens (Schmid, 1994).  Loss of genetic 
variation has also been shown to lower seed germination rates and fewer reproductive shoots, 
indicating the possibility for lowered population fitness (Williams, 2001).   Restoration projects 
incorporating plant materials from distant sources have the potential to affect adjacent native 
plant populations by introducing less fit genotypes (Potts et al., 2003; McKay et al., 2005) and 
may result in homogenization or replacement of local genotypes (Hufford and Mazer, 2003). 
Outbreeding depression resulting from genetic crossing between individuals adapted to 
different environments can threaten long-term sustainability of the restored site by reducing 
viability of the population (Hufford and Mazer, 2003).  Therefore, outbreeding depression may 
lead to an increased expression of phenotypes which are either maladapted to local conditions or 
genetically superior to local-genotype species (Lynch, 1991).  Alternatively, an increase in those 
which are genetically superior could cause a decrease in locally-adapted individuals and the 
formation of new invasive genotypes (Aubry et al., 2005).  In either case, success of the restored 
site is jeopardized.  

14 
 
For restoration to be successful, species should be both locally-adapted and genetically 
diverse (Rogers, 2004).  Genetic variation within and among populations is vital to evolutionary 
change.  Thus, maintenance of genetic diversity is a vital mechanism by which plant 
communities can adapt and evolve to survive in a changing climate (Huenneke, 1991).   
According to the Native Seed Network (www.nativeseednetwork.org), defined 
ecoregions are useful frameworks for organizing ecosystems and addressing native plant 
materials issues, and thus can be considered as a practical way of identifying seed transfer 
boundaries.  The Arizona Game and Fish Department (AZGFD) has identified six ecoregions 
within Arizona.  These ecoregions include: Colorado Plateau, Arizona-New Mexico Mountains, 
Apache Highlands North, Apache Highlands South, Sonoran Desert, and the Mohave Desert 
(Figures 2.1). 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 

15 
 
 
Figure 2.1: Arizona ecoregions and vegetative communities as identified by Arizona Game and 
Fish Department.  Map adapted by the AZGFD website (www.azgfd.gov) 
 

16 
 
III. Governmental Native Plant Policy and Programs 
Since the 1980s, policies regarding the use of native plant materials have strengthened.  
The establishment of national policies on the use of and need for native plant materials has 
provided direction for further development of federal and state native plant policies.  However, 
due to the lack of availability and cost associated with these materials, native species from non-
local sources continue to be used for revegetation of disturbed wildlands.  Because of the 
increased threat of non-native species invasions, seed certification has become an important 
preventative measure most agencies take to ensure that seed being purchased, regardless of whether 
seed is from native or non-native species, is not contaminated with noxious weeds.  Furthermore, 
the increased use of native species has lead to the establishment of alternative seed certification 
methods.   
Policies have also made available the funding needed to develop various native plant 
programs.  Currently, many of these programs have shifted their focus to conducting research on 
the development of genetically-appropriate native plant materials to be incorporated in local 
restoration projects.  With an increasing amount of research emphasizing the importance of 
genetic considerations of these materials, agencies have begun to recognize the value of using 
locally-adapted plant materials when vegetating federal and state lands, and new policies on their 
use have arisen.  Federal and state native plant policies and programs vary but share a common 
goal in protecting native plant communities from ground disturbing events (Table 3.1). 
 
 
 
 

17 
 
Federal/National Policies 
The Federal Land Policy and Management Act (FLPMA) of 1976 and the National Forest 
Management Act (NFMA) of 1976 authorized the United States Department of the Interior’s 
Bureau of Land Management (BLM) and the United States Department of Agriculture’s Forest 
Service (USFS) to plan and manage federal lands.  Specifically, NFMA was intended to protect 
plant and animal biodiversity in national forests (16 U.S.C. 1600 (section 6 (b)). These two 
federal acts helped strengthen policy direction for revegetating federal lands with native species 
(Richards et al., 1998; Soller, 2003).   
Wildfires are one of the biggest reasons for past and current restoration efforts on 
federally-administered lands within the region.  Over the last 30 years, the total amount of 
acreage burned in Arizona and New Mexico has considerably increased (Figure 3.1).  Current 
climate projections suggest that the size and intensity of wildfires will continue to increase over 
the next several decades as drought conditions persist (McKenzie et al., 2003; Westerling et al., 
2006).  Under these climate projections, the need for post-fire seeding and the use of native 
species to restore lands disturbed will continue. 
Although emergency watershed rehabilitation after wildfires began in the late 1960s, 
(Neary et al., 2000) and post-fire seeding has occurred since the 1920s, (Beyers, 2004; 
Robichaud et al., 2000), formal policies on emergency watershed rehabilitation were not 
established until the late 1980s.  Ways to minimize potential impacts on vegetation communities 
primarily for wildlife habitat and forage needs began to be addressed at this time (Neary et al., 
2000).   In the 1990s an effort began to make stabilization/rehabilitation policies more consistent 
across federal agency boundaries (GAO, 2006; Neary et al., 2000).  Today, standard policies for 
post-fire emergency stabilization/rehabilitation include: (1) evaluating the need for treatments, 

18 
 
(2) monitoring treatments, (3) repairing land and facilities damaged by fires, (4) stabilizing biotic 
communities, and (5) preventing further degradation of important cultural and natural resources 
(Neary et al., 2000; USDI and USDA, 2006).    
 
 
Figure 3.1: Total acreage burned by wildfires on all Federally-administered lands in Arizona and 
New Mexico from 1950-2007 (Sackett, Haase, and Harrington 1993; NIFC, Fire Information) 
According to Section 4.2.3 of the Interagency Burned Area Emergency Response 
Guidebook (USDI and USDA, 2006), which is an interpretation of both United States 
Department of the Interior (USDI) Department Manual 620 Chapter 3 (USDI, 2004) and United 
States Department of Agriculture (USDA) Forest Service Manual 2523 (USDA 2004), natural 
recovery of native species is preferred.  However, emergency rehabilitation treatments are 
completed after initial assessments of the burned area deem it necessary (Neary et al., 2000; 
Wirth and Pyke, 2007).  Among all post-fire rehabilitation treatments in place, grass seeding 
most often applied aerially, is the most commonly used and most cost-effective treatment to 
stabilize soils and establish ground cover (Robichaud et al., 2000; Beyers, 2004).  Non-native

19 
 
species have traditionally been used during post-fire seeding, although in recent years, new 
policies have directed federal agencies to use native species, when practical (Richards et al., 
1998; Beyers, 2004).  All seed purchased for treatments must be tested and meet the 
requirements of; (1) the Federal Seed Act (7 USC 1551-1610), (2) the state seed laws where seed 
is delivered to, and (3) Federal specification JJJ-S-181.  The use of certified seed is required if 
available, and the use of source-identified seed is recommended when native seed is collected 
from wildland sites (USDI and USDA, 2006).   
More recent policies and programs regarding the use of native plants on public lands aim 
to identify proper native seed sources, conserve biological diversity, and regulate the genetic 
sources of native seed.  In 1995, the Office of the Federal Environmental Executive (OFEE) 
issued a final guidance document to implement an April 1994 presidential executive 
memorandum on federal landscaping practices. The document states that federal grounds and 
federally funded projects should use regionally native plants for landscaping, taking place in 
mainly urban settings, to conserve the natural environment (OFEE, 1995). This memorandum is 
often interpreted as law (Bermant and Spackeen, 1997). 
Perhaps one of the most significant policies pertaining to restoration of native species 
was the signing of Executive Order 13112 in February 1999.  Executive Order 13112 established 
the National Invasive Species Council (NISC) whose primary focus is to prevent the introduction 
of invasive species, provide for their control, and to minimize the economic, ecological, and 
human health impacts caused by invasive species.  Section 2 of the Order directs all federal 
agencies to “provide for restoration of native species and habitat conditions in ecosystems that 
have been invaded by non-native species.”  To help in the effort, Congress established the 
Federal Interagency Native Plant Materials Development Committee in 2000 in order to facilitate 

20 
 
the development, supply and management of needed native plant materials.  To initiate the 
National Plant Materials Development Program, the BLM and the USFS directed $5 million and 
$10 million, respectively, in 2001.  In April 2002, a report to Congress addressed the need for 
additional funding to supply land management agencies with native plant materials for 
restoration practices and the need for an interagency native seed program (USDI and USDA, 
2002).  As a result of these efforts, several interagency programs have been established in order 
to help develop an increase supply of native plant materials.  Specifically, the Federal 
Interagency Native Plant Materials Development Program, the Great Basin Native Plant 
Selection and Increase Project (GBNSIP),  the Uncompahgre Plateau Project (UP Project), the 
Colorado Plateau Native Plant Initiative (CPNPI), and the “Seeds of Success” Program (SOS) 
have been created as an interagency effort.  These programs and initiatives have made a great 
deal of progress in research associated with native plant material development, however, actual 
development of adequate amounts of these materials have been minimal.  
As part of the Healthy Forests Initiative (16 U.S.C. 2104 Note), the BLM and the USFS 
announced guidelines in 2004 to develop and implement stewardship contracts and agreements.  
Stewardship contracts aim to improve forest health and contribute to the development of 
economies.  Guidelines help agencies achieve key land management goals, one of which is the 
reestablishment of native plant species and increased resilience to insects, disease, and other 
natural disturbances.  This enables the BLM and USFS to enter into long-term contracts (up to 
10 years) to meet land management objectives. (USDI, 15 January 2004). 
Although these policy frameworks authorize federal land management agencies to 
encourage the use of native species, practical constraints limit the degree to which federal 
agencies implement native plant policy objectives.  For example, funding restrictions often limit 

21 
 
the agencies’ capability to purchase, store, and successfully establish native seed.  Limited 
supplies and high costs associated with native plant materials make it difficult for agencies to 
purchase native plant materials when funds are insufficient or unavailable. Therefore, many of 
the following policies and programs mentioned seem to be encouraging, although agency 
changes are needed to establish more consistency between native plant policy and practice 
(Richards et al., 1998) 
 
Bureau of Land Management 
The use of native plants for restoration of disturbed wildlands is encouraged through 
various policies and programs of the BLM.  These policies and programs have enabled the BLM, 
who manages 264 million acres of public lands (Davis, 2001), to become the leader in native 
seed purchases among all the land management agencies (Southard, 2002).  Agency policy has 
established important recommendations for the use of native species on all public lands and 
helped to develop important native plant materials to restore plant communities threatened by 
invasive species. 
In accordance with the 1999 Executive Order by President Clinton, the BLM adopted 
policy for noxious weed-free seed in 2006.  Today, all seed used on lands administered by BLM 
is required to be noxious weed-free and meet certified seed quality standards.   Prior to the BLM 
purchasing or accepting seed from any source, all seed must have a valid seed test from a seed 
analyst lab by a registered analyst (BLM, 2007; Native Seed Network, 2008).  However, many 
species officially designated as federally noxious weeds are not listed on state lists (USDA, 24 
May 2008, Plants Database).  Therefore, it could be possible for weed seed that is designated as 
federally noxious to be used within the state. 

22 
 
The BLM Manual 1745 (BLM, 1992) establishes policy and guidance on the 
introduction, transplant, augmentation, and reestablishment of plants (exotic, native, and 
naturalized species) on public lands.  In assessing revegetation needs, natural recovery by native 
plant species is preferable to planting and/or seeding, although, when the potential for recovery 
of native plant species is compromised by the establishment of non-native species, planting 
and/or seeding is acceptable (BLM, 2007).  Planting and/or seeding has become a vital 
component of post-fire rehabilitation efforts and the BLM is directed to reseed burned areas with 
native plant materials which promote wildlife habitat and stabilize eroding soils (Richards et al., 
1998). 
The BLM’s native seed policies and priorities to promote ecosystem health have helped 
to encourage development of various native plant programs.   Although there are many, three of 
the largest BLM programs incorporating the use of native plants include:  Invasive and Noxious 
Weed Program, The Great Basin Restoration Initiative (GBRI), and SOS.  To help support the 
BLM’s plant policies and programs, the National Seed Warehouse located in Boise, Idaho 
purchases, tests, and stores a wide variety of native and introduced seed for several states.  The 
amount of seed each state (Idaho, Oregon, Nevada, Utah, and Colorado) can reserve is based on 
a reasonable projection of annual acreage to be stabilized/rehabilitated over a 5-year period and 
the storage capacity of the warehouse (BLM, 2007). 
To help deal with the rapid expansion of weeds across public lands, the BLM’s Noxious 
Weed Program focuses on controlling and restoring areas degraded by invasive species (BLM, 7 
Dec. 2007, Plant Conservation Program).  “Pulling Together” has become the agency’s national 
strategy for invasive species management.   This strategy calls for effective prevention and 
control of invasive plants and effective restoration of native species which help prevent 

23 
 
reinvasion of non-natives and restore ecosystem function to natural areas, agricultural lands, 
rangelands and forests.  
The GBRI is a team consisting of BLM specialists that work to provide technical 
assistance and obtain funding to support restoration of native plant communities within the Great 
Basin region.  The GBRI focuses on restoring native plant diversity and structure back into 
degraded rangelands and woodlands throughout the Great Basin region.  By doing this it hopes to 
improve resiliency of plant communities and provide critical wildlife and livestock habitat within 
the region.  The GBRI also has encouraged the initiation of smaller, similar projects within the 
Great Basin (Pellant, 2008) including the Great Basin Native Plant Selection and Increase Project 
which aims to increase the availability of native plant materials for restoration of degraded 
rangelands across the Great Basin (GBRI, 2001).  
In 2001, the BLM initiated a native seed collection effort as part SOS.  It has become the 
core of the National Plant Materials Development Program.  The SOS is an ongoing effort aimed 
at improving the health and productivity of public lands through collection, conservation, and 
development of native plant materials used for restoration within the United States.  The BLM 
collections teams, one of which includes the Arboretum at Flagstaff in northern Arizona, gather 
wildland-collected seed for both long-term conservation and immediate restoration needs. The 
program provides native seed from many species of plants to growers, researchers, and 
administrators of seed in the United States (BLM, 2006). 
Within the states of Utah, Nevada, and now Colorado, the BLM has received enough 
demand for seed collection permits that it has developed or is currently developing statewide 
guidance which standardizes seed collection procedures and pricing lists (BLM, 2003).  All BLM 
offices authorize commercial harvesting of native seed and require a permit to collect on public 

24 
 
lands.  Collection must take place in areas outside national monuments, Areas of Critical 
Environmental Concern, and wilderness areas (BLM, 2005). There is currently no national seed 
harvesting guidance which stipulates regulations on wildland collected seed for commercial sale.  
 
National Park Service 
The National Park Service (NPS) may manage native plant species if deemed necessary 
in order to protect park resources in landscapes affected by natural and non-natural disturbances 
and if the practices used will not cause damage to the species or the ecosystem which they rely 
on.  In such instances, restoration and revegetation efforts include the use of seeds, cuttings, or 
transplants representing species as closely related genetically and ecologically to park 
populations as possible and preferable from populations which are in nearest proximity to the 
project area.  Use of exotic species is prohibited within the park and displacement of native 
species by exotics is discouraged (NPS, 2006). 
 In 1996, NPS outlined a Strategic Plan for Managing Invasive Non-native Plants on 
National Park System Lands.  According to the plan, vegetation projects must use locally-grown 
native plant materials where available to maintain genetic and ecological integrity of native 
ecosystems (NPS, 1996).  Programs are in place to provide restoration materials that are specific 
each park.  These programs support greenhouses and gardens which are used to develop locally-
adapted native plant materials which help manage population gene pools (NPS, 1996; NPS, 
2006).  
Harvest of designated species of plants by the public may be allowed on NPS land for an 
authorized purpose or to meet management needs.  The NPS must provide follow-up monitoring 
to determine the impacts of the harvesting activities and ensure that such removals will not cause 

25 
 
unacceptable impacts on natural processes and park resources in order for harvesting to be 
permitted.  Harvest of certain plant parts for personal consumption is specifically authorized by 
the superintendent in accordance with 36 CFR 2.1(c)(1) (NPS, 2006). 
 
Bureau of Indian Affairs 
Although the Bureau of Indian Affairs does not have a specific agency policy focusing 
the use of native plant materials, the BIA must follow the USDI Department Manual 620, 
Chapter 3 and Section 4.2.3 in the Interagency Burn Area Emergency Response Guidebook 
when applying post-fire stabilization seeding treatments.   Seeding treatments may be applied 
only if seeding treatments act to (1) stabilize the site and minimize water or wind erosion, (2) 
reduce the invasion of non-native invasive plants, or (3) prevent critical habitat for federal listed 
threatened or endangered species from being more impaired than if nothing was done (Tom 
Branson, BIA, Personal communication; USDI and USDA, 2006). 
 
USDA Forest Service 
The USFS policies began to address the use of native plant materials for revegetation 
purposes in the 1980s.  Since that time major strides have been made to improve the agency’s 
native plant policies.  Resource managers are now incorporating the use of native species into 
restoration/rehabilitation efforts in order to mitigate effects of mining, road construction and 
maintenance, recreation, and other ground-disturbing activities on native plant communities 
(USFS, 2004).  Programs have been made available to support the increased use of native plant 
materials. 
In 2006, the USFS began developing a new proposed directive to Forest Service Manual 
(FSM) 2070, which addresses the use of native plant materials in the revegetation, restoration, 

26 
 
and rehabilitation of National Forest System lands (USFS, 2006).  The USFS issued a new final 
directive (FSM 2000) to FSM 2070 for native plant materials on February 13, 2008.  FSM 2000 
directs collaboration with federal, state, and local government entities and the public to develop 
and implement actions to increase the availability of native plant materials.  The policy states 
that native plant materials should be the first choice in revegetation of native ecosystems where 
natural regeneration will not occur.  This policy also stresses the importance of genetically 
appropriate native plant materials for restoration (USFS, 2008). 
Recent changes in the Burned Area Emergency Rehabilitation (BAER) policy provide 
direction to restore burned areas with native species when native seed is readily available after 
wildfires.  According to FSM 2523, natural recovery by native species is preferred although 
seeding or planting of grass, forbs, shrubs, or trees can be implemented for emergency 
stabilization, to restore ecosystem structure and function, or to prevent invasion of invasive 
species (USFS, October 2004).  For both short and long-term fire recovery efforts, USFS policies 
encourage the use of native seed, but only where it is “feasible, practical, and affordable” 
(Richards et al., 1998).  
The USFS has been at the forefront in advancing the development of critical restoration 
species.  Since the development of the National Seed Laboratory (NSL) has shifted its focus 
from production of predominately conifer species to restore erodible farm lands to conservation 
and production of native grasses and forbs for habitat restoration (Ledig, 1988; Karrfalt, 2006).  
The NSL focuses its attention on a variety of seed issues including seed dormancy, zoning, 
production, and cleaning.  Its efforts have produced needed protocols for native plant materials 
relating to seed collection, cleaning and long-term storage (Karrfalt, 2006). 

27 
 
The USFS also conducts several rehabilitation and restoration programs to restore 
ecosystem sustainability on Forest Service lands.  Specifically, the USFS Botany Program 
focuses on conservation and management of rare plants, conducting vegetation inventories, and 
increasing production and use of native plant materials for restoration, revegetation, and 
rehabilitation projects on their managed lands.  The Native Plant Materials Program focuses on 
the latter and helps select and develop these materials by determining appropriate plant selection, 
collection techniques, propagation techniques, storage, and planting methodologies (USFS, 
2008).  The National Strategy and Implementation Plan for Invasive Species Management 
incorporates using native or desired non-native materials and prioritizing and developing native 
plant stock that is resistant to invasive pests and pathogens.  This Plan also serves as guidance for 
USFS invasive species management programs that are designed to control, manage, and 
eliminate (where possible) established infestations and reintroduce native plants to restore the 
vegetation (USFS, October 2004). 
The USFS currently sells native seed for harvest under the definition of Special Forest 
Products or Forest Botanical Products using the same regulations as timber sales following 
regulations specified under 36 CFR Chapter 2 Part 223.  Because of recent increases in the sale 
of forest products, a proposed rule to add subparts G and H to 36 CFR Part 223 was put forth in 
the Federal Register on October 22, 2007 concerning sales of forest botanical products (Kim 
Newbauer, USDA Forest Service, Personal Communication).  The proposed rule formally 
establishes a pilot program which charges and collects fees associated with the harvest and sale 
of forest botanical products on national forest lands.  This proposed rule also aims to ensure 
sustainable harvest of those products (Sale and Disposal of National Forest System Timber, 
2007). 

28 
 
Various types of seed harvesting may occur on USFS land; and permit types, costs, and 
stipulations vary by National Forest and Region.  For small scale harvesting activities, the 
purchaser of the special forest product asks permission to harvest, enters into a contract with the 
USFS, or buys a Special Forest Products Permit.  The USFS conducts a competitive bidding 
process for sales of special forest products valued at $10,000 or more (Sale and Disposal of 
National Forest System Timber, 2007).  In either case a review process takes place to ensure that 
National Environmental Protection Act requirements are satisfied and that there are no negative 
impacts associated with the harvesting activity (Kim Newbauer, USDA Forest Service, Personal 
Communication). 
The USFS does not permit collection to occur on sites within off-limit areas, such as in 
Research Natural Areas, sensitive or federally listed plant sites, or other environmentally 
sensitive areas and highly discourages collection of seed in areas occupied by invasive plant 
species.   While harvesting, the amount of seed taken from a given site is limited to no more than 
50 percent of the seed crop in order to encourage natural recruitment of native populations.  The 
USFS also stipulates that collection should be from a minimum of five collection sites at least 
0.5-1.0 mile apart (USFS, 25 March 2008, Celebrating Wildflowers). 
 
Natural Resource Conservation Service 
In 1935 the USDA Natural Resources Conservation Service (NRCS), then the Soil 
Conservation Service (SCS), was created in order to deal with the severe soil erosion issues of 
the Dust Bowl Era (Helms, 1992; USDI and USDA, 2002) and the need for plant materials to 
rehabilitate soil ridden lands.  Just two years after, President Roosevelt recommended 
legislation that would allow local landowners to form soil conservation districts. The NRCS 

29 
 
has since developed national standards stressing the use of native species, continued the 
success of the NRCS Plant Materials Centers (PMCs), and created numerous incentive 
programs which increase the use of native species.  Today, nearly 3,000 conservation districts 
have been formed and coordinate involvement between NRCS and local landowners. 
The NRCS National Conservation Practice Standards (CPS) sets forth the minimum 
quality criteria that must be met during the application of all conservation practices.  Most CPS’s 
for vegetation improvement practices emphasize the use of native species when they are 
available.  For example, the CPS for Conservation Cover states that certified seed and 
appropriate native planting stock that is site specific should be used when it is available to meet 
the management objective of reestablishing the native plant community (NRCS, June 2007).  The 
revised standard for Restoration and Management of Declining Habitats provides guidance for 
restoring and conserving rare or declining native plant communities and associated wildlife 
species. 
Plant Materials Centers (PMC) were established throughout the United States with the 
primary focus of providing plant materials to meet conservation program needs (NRCS, 2005).  
By the 1990’s PMC began emphasizing the use of native plant materials for restoration projects 
(Helms, 1992; USDI and USDA, 2002).  The PMC have become the cornerstone of the NRCS 
Plant Materials Program which aims to develop and provide essential knowledge and plant 
materials to solve conservation issues and reestablish ecosystem function on degraded lands. 
Through extensive research the Program develops essential planting and revegetation techniques 
and tests the use of native species to control noxious weeds.  The Program includes a network of 
27 PMCs and associated Plant Materials Specialists and partnered with other federal and state 
agencies as well as non-profit groups to develop needed plant materials and management 

30 
 
practices for current conservation needs (NRCS, 2005).  Through research and development, 
PMCs have supplied new growers with high quality seed stocks.  Within the last 70 years the 
program has released over 600 conservation species, most being grown by commercial growers 
(Undayag and Englert, 2001).  
Several important programs of the Farm Security and Rural Investment Act of 2002 
(Farm Bill) gives cost-share payments to protect and restore natural communities.  These 
programs strongly stress the importance in increased usage of native plant materials (NRCS, 
2002).  For example, the Wildlife Habitat Incentives Program (WHIP) is a voluntary program 
which provides up to 75 percent cost-share assistance to landowners who want to improve and 
protect wildlife habitat on private and Tribal lands.  By 2004, nearly 2.3 million acres were 
enrolled in the program.  Most of WHIP funding has gone toward improving upland wildlife 
habitat, such as native prairie (NRCS, September 2004).  The Wetlands Reserve Program (WRP) 
is a similar program which offers landowners a payment of up to 75 percent of the cost 
restoration activities which protect, restore, and enhance wetlands on their property.  In 2007, 
approximately 1,899,979 acres were enrolled in the program (NRCS, April 2007).  The 
Environmental Quality Incentives Program (EQIP) is perhaps one of the largest NRCS programs 
with more than 51.5 million acres enrolled in the program.  The EQIP concentrates on improving 
water quality, conserving both ground and surface water, reducing soil erosion from cropland 
and forestland, and improving rangeland.  A payment of up to $450,000 can be given for each 
EQIP contract and since 1996 has obligated $1.08 million dollars to those in the program 
(NRCS, October 2004).   Finally, the Grasslands Reserve Program (GRP) helps landowners 
restore and protect grassland, rangeland, pastureland, shrubland and provides assistance for 

31 
 
rehabilitating grasslands.  This program has enrolled 909,100 acres and has made available $180 
million in financial assistance (NRCS, March 2006). 
Conservation Districts work in conjunction with NRCS but are organized under state and 
tribal law and are directed by locally elected directors or supervisors.  These Districts evaluate 
the conservation needs of their respective areas and create unique partnerships between federal, 
state and local agencies to address local natural resource concerns.  Districts promote and 
provide voluntary compliance with environmental laws by encouraging “best management 
practices.”  
Within the State of Arizona, Natural Resources Conservation Districts (NRCDs) are run 
as a program administered by the State Land Department.  The NRCDs promote the restoration 
and the conservation of land and soil resources within the state.  The NRCD activities include 
soil, water and natural resource conservation, watershed management programs, the protection of 
open space and farmland, and integrated land use planning.  The NRCDs are in partnership with 
NRCS Offices, and play an important role in EQIP by identifying local resources concerns and 
priorities for the grants.  Many are involved with educational centers and have programs 
incorporating at least some use of native plant materials (JLBC, 2007, Project Summary). 
 
Federal Highway Administration 
The Federal Highway Administration (FHWA) has policies for the use native plant 
materials are in place, however, these policies do not require that all federal-aid highway projects 
use native plant materials and instead have made their use an incentive to receive additional 
funding.  Conversely, some programs are available help increase the use of native plant materials 

32 
 
on all FHWA roadways, while other programs make use of “wildflowers” which may not 
necessarily mean those species which are native.   
In 1998, a revised guidance for the native wildflower planting requirement in response to 
the Surface Transportation and Uniform Relocation Assistance Act of 1987 (STURAA), requires 
that native wildflower seeds and/or seedlings be planted as part of all landscaping projects 
undertaken on the federal-aid highway system.  At least one-quarter of one percent of funds 
expended for landscaping projects must be used to plant native wildflowers on the project site 
unless a waiver has been granted.  The native wildflower planting requirement is applicable to 
only landscaping projects which are meant to improve esthetics of the highway and not to every 
federal-aid highway construction project.  Nevertheless, specifications recommend that native 
wildflower and grass seed mixes be collected and/or grown within the project region and be site 
specific.  Maintaining species diversity is a priority on all projects (Harper-Lore and Wilson, 
2000).  Section 6006 of the Safe, Accountable, Flexible, Efficient Transportation Equity Act: A 
Legacy for Users (SAFETEA-LU) of 2005 provides new and expanded eligibility for the use of 
federal-aid funds for projects that promote the control of noxious weeds and the use of native 
plant materials for revegetation (FHWA, 2005).  
In following the guidance set forth by the Executive Memorandum on Beneficial 
Landscaping and Executive Order 13112 the FHWA maintains a Roadside Vegetation Program.  
This program acts as a holistic approach to roadside management and incorporates the use of 
native vegetation to improve water quality and erosion control, increases wildlife habitat and 
control noxious weeds all roadsides (Harper-Lore and Wilson, 2000).  Meanwhile, FHWAs 
Operation Wildflower Program, while sounding beneficial, may introduce species that are not 
necessarily native to the project area.  This program is a volunteer cooperative agreement 

33 
 
between the National Council of State Garden Clubs, Inc., state highway agencies, and FHWA 
which allows state garden clubs to pay for or furnish wildflower seeds or seedlings to a state 
highway agency for planting within the highway right-of-way.  Federal funds are available for 
participation in the cost of planting and maintaining wildflowers supplied by state garden clubs 
and other organizations along roadsides (FHWA, 24 February 2003, Wildflowers).   
 
Arizona State Mandates 
 
No general native seed policy exists within the State of Arizona, but specific policies 
exist for seed labeling requirements and include labeling for native seed.  Within the Arizona 
Revised Statues, the Arizona Seed Law mentions only agricultural, vegetable, and ornamental 
seeds, although some laws may still apply to native seed.  In accordance with §3-235 of the 
Arizona Seed Law, an Arizona seed dealer or an out-of-state seed dealer who sells, 
distributes, processes, or mixes seed for the use of others must obtain a license from the 
environmental services division of the Arizona department of agriculture.  §3-237 of the 
Arizona Seed Law specifies seed labeling requirements which include: (1) germination, (2) 
purity, (3) noxious weed content, (4) precautionary statements the director considers to be 
necessary, (5) labeler or packager information, and (6) seed origin. 
 
As a funding requirement of the State Wildlife Grants program, Congress charged each of 
the 56 States and Territories with developing a statewide “Comprehensive Wildlife Conservation 
Strategy” (CWCS).  Arizona’s CWCS is a comprehensive document that plans for conserving 
fish, wildlife, and wildlife habitats over a ten year period (2005-2015).  There are eight required 
elements of the CWCS.  The fourth element of the CWCS describes conservation strategies to 
conserve habitats which will benefit wildlife communities.  One specific strategy calls for efforts 

34 
 
which will limit the spread of invasive plants and promote the restoration of native vegetation in 
disturbed areas (AZGFD, 2006). 
In 2007, the Arizona Invasive Species Advisory Council (AISAC) began developing a 
comprehensive statewide invasive species management plan for Arizona and is to be completed 
in June 2008.  One of the objectives of the Council is to recommend specific actions/needs to 
state agencies including the Arizona Game and Fish Department, and the Arizona Department of 
Agriculture, regarding prevention, response, control, management, restoration, and other areas 
based on the prioritization (AISAC, 2007). 
 
Arizona Game and Fish Department 
 
The Arizona Game and Fish Department (AZGFD) is a state entity, and, therefore, must 
follow all policies set forth by the State of Arizona (mentioned above).  A major task of the 
AZGFD is to conserve, enhance, and restore native wildlife and their associated habitats.  Use of 
native plant species for restoration is of major importance.  Although the AZGFD receives no 
money from the state’s general fund like most other states agencies, the Arizona Revised Statutes 
§17-405 of the Arizona Game and Fish Laws and Rules allows AZGFD to accept federal aid and 
grant funding for restoration and wildlife habitat restoration and enhancement projects (AZGFD, 
2007).   The AZGFD receives the majority of its funding for habitat restoration projects through 
Senate Bill 1441 (SB 1441) Wildlife Habitat Restoration and Enhancement Funds.  Fund monies 
are appropriated by the state legislature as a give-back for money taken out of the AZGFD 
budget in a previous deficit cycle.  The AZGFD also uses Wildlife Conservation Fund revenues 
for these projects.  Other monies for restoration projects are appropriated through the Pittman-
Robertson/Dingell-Johnson (PRDJ) Special Line Item.  The PRDJ provides the state’s matching 

35 
 
funds for federal funds used for wildlife habitat restoration projects, hunter education programs, 
and wildlife population surveys (JLBS, 2007, AZGFD).  The Heritage Fund is also critical in 
providing the Department with the needed funds to assist in the recovering of Arizona’s native 
wildlife and associated habitats (AZGFD, 2 May 2008, Heritage Fund Program). 
 
Arizona Department of Transportation 
Projects located within Arizona Department of Transportation (ADOT) jurisdiction must 
develop and implement a Stormwater Pollution Protection Plan (SWPPP) which includes 
completed Permanent Pollution Prevention Best Management Practices (BMPs).  Described 
within the Permanent Pollution Prevention BMPs is the reestablishment of vegetation to 
disturbed soils.  Revegetation must be conducted in accordance with the criteria established in 
ADOT Stored Specification 805SEED: Item 8050003 Seeding (ADOT, 3 December 2007, 
Stormwater Requirements for External Parties).  Work under ADOT Stored Specification 
805SEED consists of furnishing all revegetation materials, preparing the soil, applying Class II 
seed (seed for range grass, flowers or shrubs with mulching), and establishing seed on all 
disturbed or non-vegetated areas.  Seed supplied to ADOT must be tested and labeled in 
accordance with the Arizona Revised Statues and the USDA rules and regulations under the 
Federal Seed Act (ADOT, 2005).  A uniform perennial vegetative cover with a density of 70% of 
the native background vegetative cover must be established in order for projects to met the 
requirement for “Final Stabilization” and submit a Notice of Termination (ADOT, 2006). 
Beginning in 1992 ADOT required the use of only native species for revegetation on 
disturbed project areas.  All seeding is completed by subcontractors.  Before the seed is applied 

36 
 
to the project area, ADOT checks that the environment condition from which the seed is 
collected matches that of the site (LeRoy Brady, ADOT, Pers comm.). 
Seed Certification – Arizona Crop Improvement Association 
Demand for native plant materials has led to an increase in seed certification, particularly 
in the western United States.  The purpose of seed certification is to verify source of the seed, 
preserve genetic purity and identity, and prevent the spread of noxious weeds.  Seed certifcation 
has become a vital component to the increased success of revegetation projects. The Arizona 
Crop Improvement Association, Inc. (ACIA) is designated as an official seed certifying agency 
for Arizona in accordance to §3-232 of the Arizona Seed Law.   The ACIA’s seed certifying 
activities meet or exceed the standards and procedures specified in Part 201.68 through 201.78 of 
the Federal Seed Act (7 U.S.C. 1551 (a)(25) (ACIA, 2007).  As an official member of the 
Association of Official Seed Certifying Agencies (AOSCA), ACIA is given the authority to 
certify seed within its jurisdictions according to AOSCA standards (AOSCA, 2007) and only 
those crop varieties that are accepted by the ACIA, in accordance with the criteria established by 
the AOSCA, are eligible for certification.   
Because of the increasing demand for native plant materials for restoration, revegetation 
and stabilization of natural communities, AOSCA has implemented certification requirements 
and standards that accommodate plant germplasm (those not yet released as formal varieties) of 
native grasses, forbs and woody plants.  As an alternative to formal variety release, AOSCA Pre-
Variety Germplasm (PVG) categories facilitate procurement, production, and distribution of 
plant germplasm materials (AOSCA, Native Plant Connection Bulletin).  Pre-Variety 
Germplasm certification has became essential in assuring genetic integrity of native seeds on the 
market, and is an important tool used to preserve native plant populations.  The Arizona Crop

Improvement Association has adopted all AOSCA standards for certification of plant germplasm 
types. 
The AOSCA seed certification agencies require seed collectors/producers to follow 
specified requirements and procedures which vary depending on whether the certification is for 
wildland collected seed or for field/nursery production (AOSCA, Native Plant Connection 
Bulletin).  Germplasm originating from a wildland stand is assigned a genetic status of either 

39 
 
“manipulated-track” or “natural-track” (Figure 3.2).  Germplasm accessions that are an 
unrestricted representation of an intact wildland plant population on the original site follow 
natural-track certification procedures.  When natural-track germplasm is increased in the field, 
genetic manipulation the species must be avoided.  Germplasm accessions which are 
purposefully or inadvertently hybridized with other accessions or selected for distinctive traits 
within the population are routed to the manipulated-track (AOSCA, Native Plant Connection 
Bulletin). 
 According to  AOSCA certification standards a race or ecotype of native or naturalized 
species following either track may be categorized into one of three PVG classes: (1) Source 
Identified Class (unevaluated germplasm identified only as to species and location of the wild 
growing parents), (2) Selected Class Seed (germplasm showing promise of desirable traits, 
having been selected either within or as a common site comparison among accessions or 
populations of the same species), and (3) Tested Class Seed (germplasm for which progeny 
testing has proven desirable traits to be heritable).  The progression through the PVG categories 
may lead to formal Variety/Cultivar release, which includes seed certification generation 
designations of Breeder (seed directly controlled by the originating or sponsoring plant breeding 
organization), Founder (the progeny of Breeder or Foundation seed handled to maintain specific 
genetic purity and identity), Registered (the progeny of Breeder or Foundation seed handled to 
maintain satisfactory genetic purity and identity), and Certified seed (the progeny of Breeder, 
Foundation or Registered seed handled to maintain satisfactory genetic purity and identity) 
(ACIA, 2007; AOSCA, Native Plant Connection Bulletin).  Seeds of all weeds classified as 
“noxious” (seeds of perennial or annual weeds which, when established, are highly destructive 
and difficult to control by ordinary good cultural practices) are prohibited by ACIA standards 

40 
 
from any class of certified seed.  Pre-Variety Germplasm tags are color coded according to 
category with specific generation information listed on the PVG tag; yellow for Source Identified 
Seed, green for Selected Seed, and blue for Tested Seed.  Variety/Cultivar tags are color coded to 
signify generation; white for Breeder and Foundation, purple for Registered, and blue for 
Certified.  (AOSCA, Native Plant Connection Bulletin).   
It appears that the seed certification process is a practical way for seed buyers to be 
assured genetic identity and purity of plant materials of either variety or cultivar release.  
Although the process of PVG certification is relatively new, PVG certified seed have been 
incorporated into revegetation projects across the West.  Seed warehouses including those 
operated by the BLM located in Ephraim, Utah and Boise, Idaho continue to increase seed 
storage of PVG certified seed. 
Cost associated with seed field inspection and certification vary depending on the type of 
certification, size of field, distance the field inspector must travel, pounds of seed, and the 
number of lots to be certified (Table 3.2).   The ACIA is a non-profit organization, operated 
solely on membership and service fees.  Individuals requiring field inspection services pay an 
annual membership fee before services are rendered.   Field inspection fees are charged on a per 
acre basis and require reimbursement of travel expenses (mileage/lodging) to be paid when 
necessary.  Depending on the type of certification desired (Variety/Cultivar or PVG) seeds are 
either sent to a certified lab for testing or immediately bagged according to PVG certification 
type.  All Variety/Cultivar seed harvested for legal sale must be tested by an approved seed 
testing laboratory in order to verify seed germination and purity as well as the presence of weed 
seeds and other seed factors required for the specified label.
