In summation, native plant policies have strengthened since the 1980’s, and the increased 
threat of non-native species invasions and the need to protect the genetic integrity of native palnt 
population has made the use of native species and seed certification important.   However, these 
policies seem to be inconsistent across agency boundaries.  As large-scale disturbances continue 
to increase within the West, the use of locally-adapted plant materials will be vital in restoring 
native plant community structure, function, and resilience to a changing climate.  In addition, 

43 
 
ecological research appears to have a strong influence on native seed policy.  Federal and state 
programs have been an effective method in providing the research needed to initiate policy 
changes.  Therefore, it would be useful to appropriate funds to increase programs which conduct 
research relating to the development of locally-native plant materials.    A focus needs to be 
made on making native plant and seed certification policies more consistent among agencies.   
 
IV. Assessment of Current and Future Native Plant Materials Market Concerns and Needs 
  In recent years, large-scale restoration projects have increased due to an increase in 
disturbance events throughout northern Arizona.  Federal, state, private, and non-profit 
organizations are experiencing the need for locally-adapted native plant materials to restore these 
disturbed areas.  Although research has increased the amount of information and tools available 
for native plant material production, there remains a limited supply of native plant materials in 
northern Arizona.  Stronger policies emphasizing the use of native plant materials have 
encouraged collaborative efforts among agencies and organizations to carry out continued 
research and development of native plant materials. These groups have acted as models which 
helped to create regional native plant development efforts and have provided information 
important to the initiation of a native plant market within northern Arizona. However, demand 
and supply surveys developed to assess the opportunity for market initiation within the region 
identified various limiting factors to consider before moving forward with its development.  
Furthermore, although native plant cooperatives and groups outside northern Arizona act as 
important models, the region’s extreme environmental conditions pose major challenges to 
native plant production, and a more diverse approach to initiating a native plant market may be 
needed.   

44 
 
Current and Ongoing Restoration Projects in Northern Arizona 
Over the last several years, the number of large-scale restoration projects within northern 
Arizona has increased.   Many of the projects have been initiated to restore ecologically degraded 
landscapes, and a heavy focus has been placed on improving native plant and wildlife 
communities’ structure and function.  Federal, state, private, and non-profit entities have 
partnered to pool the resources needed to initiate local restoration efforts.  Excluding post-fire 
rehabilitation efforts, over 29,500 acres have been or are in the process of being restored and/or 
improved since 2005.   Total restoration project acreage is undoubtedly higher as estimates on 
some projects were not available for this study.  
 
Bureau of Land Management 
As a result of several major fires occurring within the Mojave Desert in 2005 and 2006, 
the BLM took action to accelerate vegetative recovery in burned Mojave Desert Tortoise 
(Gopherus agassizii) critical habitat through a variety of treatments.  Rehabilitation of the 
105,083 acres burned within the Pakoon Basin, located within Grand Canyon-Parashant National 
Monument in northwest Arizona, has been a high priority to the BLM Arizona Strip District.  In 
2006, the BLM aerially applied a seed mix of native species to 34,099 burned acres in Pakoon 
Basin.  In 2007, approximately 8,389 acres were reseeded, and the BLM incorporated seed on 
158 acres of the reseeded areas by dragging small bushy trees and shrubs over the seeded areas.  
All seed purchased for the project came from several different vendors at a national seed buy.  
Monitoring sites have been developed to evaluate effectiveness of seeding (Kathleen Harcksen, 
BLM, pers comm.).   
 

45 
 
United States Fish and Wildlife Service 
The United States Fish and Wildlife Service (USFWS) has been involved with the BLM 
in the Pakoon Springs Restoration Project located within Pakoon Basin.  Pakoon Springs is the 
largest collection of springs within the Grand Canyon-Parashant National Monument.  This 
project involves approximately 70 to 80 acres of restoration work to rehabilitate and enhance 
native biodiversity, ecological function, and natural flow regimes back into the drainage and 
reestablishment of cottonwoods willows around several ponds.   
The USFWS is also working within the Glen Canyon National Recreation Area on a 
proposed project to remove tamarisk at one or more sites on the Colorado River.  They will 
attempt to replace disturbed areas with native cottonwoods and willows (William Austin, 
USFWS, pers comm.). 
 
Grand Canyon National Park 
Current projects incorporating the use of native plant materials inside Grand Canyon 
National Park (GCNP) consist mostly of revegetation in areas disturbed by roadway construction 
activities.   Native plant materials collected within the park are used to mitigate negative effects 
of construction activities on native vegetation.  Exotic species removal also plays an important 
role in restoring native ecosystems within the park.  Ideally, areas where exotics have been 
removed should be revegetated with native species.  However, the amount of materials needed 
for revegetation of these areas not available.  The GCNP is currently working on increasing 
production of native plant materials and developing a reliable native seed source.  In order to 
accomplish this goal, there is an increased effort for collection of native seed grown in native 
seed fields (Janice Busco, GCNP, pers comm.). 

46 
 
Arizona Game and Fish Department 
Over the last several years the AZGFD has initiated a variety of wildlife habitat 
improvement projects in northern Arizona.  In 2007, declining mule deer winter range quality, 
specifically the reduction of critical winter browse shrubs led to the implementation of the 
Westside Winter Range Project on the North Kaibab Ranger District.   This project has 
implemented several treatment types focusing on seed bed preparation, minimizing soil 
disturbance, and follow-up weed treatments.  During October 2005 to April 2007, 24,000 acres 
were cleared and 500 acres were seeded with four locally collected shrub species: cliffrose 
(Pushia Mexicana var. stansburiana), sagebrush (Artemisia spp.), fourwing saltbush (Atriplex 
canescens), and winterfat (Krascheninnikovia lanata) to help improve mule deer winter forage 
species.  Approximately 250 acres of follow-up herbicide treatments were completed.  An 
additional 900 acres are to be seeded with locally collected shrubs in 2008.  This project is likely 
to continue for 5-10 years and will involve multiple research and monitoring projects to evaluate 
effectiveness of treatments within the seeded acreage (Andi Rogers, AZGFD, Personal 
Communication). 
Since 2002, woodland/grassland restoration treatments have been ongoing in the 
Anderson Mesa area which is located just southeast of Flagstaff.  As a part of the Anderson Mesa 
Pronghorn Antelope Management Plan which incorporates collaboration among agency 
personnel, non-profit organizations, private citizens, and students, the AZGFD’s Anderson Mesa 
Habitat Project aims to improve winter habitat range for declining pronghorn populations (Clark, 
2006).  Seeding of native shrub species within the project area by the AZGFD started in 2006.  
To date, the AZGFD has seeded approximately 555 acres within burn pile sites on private land.  
Total acreage seeded with native species within the greater Anderson Mesa area since 2002 by 

47 
 
both the AZGFD and USFS equals approximately 2,908 acres (Diablo Trust, 2005). This project 
is expected to continue until fall 2009.   
Additionally, approximately two acres of restoration and forage seeding is expected to 
take place at Raymond Wildlife Area during the summer of 2008.  The seeded areas will serve as 
plots which would then act as an additional source of native seed to be used on future restoration 
projects within the region (Rick Miller, AZGFD, Personal Communication).  Furthermore, the 
AZGFD Habitat Program has been also working towards restoration of native vegetation within 
post-fire burns crucial to wintering mule deer on the Kaibab National Forest. 
 
Arizona Department of Transportation  
Since 2003, non-native species populations have grown significantly along ADOT rights-
of-way, specifically I-17 and I-40.   From January 1, 2006 to June 30, 2006, 912 acres of noxious 
weeds were treated in northern Arizona.  This showed an increase from the 875 acres treated 
during the same time in 2005 and 481 acres treated during that time in 2004.  Arizona 
Department of Transportation continues to monitor and treat infestations annually by hand 
pulling, biological controls, herbicides, and native plant seeding through its Integrated Weed 
Management Program (ADOT, 2 May 2008, Current Flagstaff Projects).  Every year ADOT 
oversees construction, reconstruction, and maintenance of approximately 6,000 12’ lane miles 
within the Flagstaff and Holbrook Districts alone (ADOT, 2 May 2008, District Information).  
Due to current policies, certified native seed is used to revegetate all areas which have been 
disturbed due to work associated with these roadways.  
 

48 
 
Northern Arizona University 
The Ecological Restoration Institute (ERI), an independent research branch of Northern 
Arizona University (NAU), completes ecologically based restoration in order to reestablish 
ecosystem function of Southwestern forests.  The ERI is an active member in the Northern 
Arizona Native Seed Alliance and is involved in several studies incorporating the use and need 
for native plant materials.  Northern Arizona University also presents students with an 
opportunity to engage in ecologically-based research within the region, some of which involves 
restoration.  Specifically, studies that incorporate the use of native plant materials include 
riparian restoration, landscaping, and effects of seeding on disturbed ecosystems are currently in 
place.  
 
Grand Canyon Trust 
The Grand Canyon Trust’s (GCT) Restoration Program is among several other programs 
which act to protect and restore the Colorado Plateau.  This program has initiated a multitude of 
projects to help restore native plant communities within the region including: invasive species 
removal, wildlife habitat improvement, fire rehabilitation, understory vegetation management, 
watershed management, and restoration of native plant and wildlife communities within Kane 
and Two Mile Ranches.  In 2005, the GCT and The Conservation Fund jointly purchased Kane 
and Two Mile ranches located north of the Grand Canyon at the eastern end of the Arizona Strip.   
Both ranches have presented the GCT with the opportunity to expand on ground restoration and 
science-based research. The GCT’s activities within ponderosa pine forests, shrublands, and 
grasslands of the southern Colorado Plateau focus on restoring composition, structure, and 
natural processes these ecosystem types (GCT, 2 May 2008, Restoration Programs). 

49 
 
   Arizona Native Plant Society 
 
The Arizona Native Plant Society (AZNPS) has recently become involved in the Plant 
Atlas of Arizona Project.  This project is a statewide effort to gather scientific documentation of 
plant diversity throughout Arizona.  Research will build on current botanical collections and 
focus on areas where collections have been limited.  The project hopes to provide more accurate 
and detailed geographic information on the flora within Arizona to the public and land managers.  
To assist in the effort, AZNPS members will be trained on techniques used to collect voucher 
specimens of native plants and record field data throughout the state.  The botanical information 
obtained will help restorationists identify specific location of plant species within the state (Jessa 
Fisher, AZNPS, pers comm.). 
 
Nature Conservancy 
        
The Nature Conservancy’s Hart Prairie Preserve, located in northern Arizona, 
encompasses 245 acres and contains one the largest rare Bebb willow (Salix bebbiana) 
communities in the United States.  Therefore, goals of Hart Prairie aim to not only enhance 
viability of this rare stand, but also to conserve and restore ecological health of the preserve as a 
whole.  Native plant seeding has been done in the past for rehabilitation of disturbed areas within 
the Preserve.  Conservation work at Hart Prairie Preserve “is scheduled to continue for the 
foreseeable future” (TNC, 2 May 2008, Conservation Work at Hart Prairie Preserve).   
 
The Greater Flagstaff Forests Partnership           
The Greater Flagstaff Forests Partnership (GFFP), a cooperative effort with the USFS, is 
dedicated to restoring ecological health of northern Arizona forests.  During the next ten years, 

50 
 
GFFP will undertake annual projects to restore forest health and reduce fire risk within the 
Flagstaff Urban Wildland Interface (approximately 180,000 acres).  Future understory restoration 
projects taken on by the GFFP hope to emphasize the reestablishment of native plant 
communities (GFFP, 2 May 2008, A Guide to the Greater Flagstaff Forests).  
 
Current Native Plant Materials Availability in Northern Arizona 
Native plant material availability within northern Arizona is limited, and no large-scale 
seed companies exist within the region.  Only a hand-full of small local nurseries are currently in 
the business of selling native plant materials and native plant seed.  Flagstaff Native Plant & 
Seed (FNPS) in Flagstaff, Arizona currently sells locally-adapted native plant materials which 
are adapted to the Colorado Plateau, some of which are source-identified.   Flagstaff Native Plant 
& Seed caters to both wholesale and retail markets, however, the majority of its wholesale 
products go to landscapers, and only minimal quantities (less than 5%) are sold to the USFS and 
NPS for restoration purposes (Nigel Sparks, FNPS, Personal communication).  Other nurseries in 
Flagstaff and Clarkdale sell native plant materials which may or may not come from local 
genetic sources.  Overall, the amount of native plant materials available is only adequate to 
support small-scale (less than 25 acres) projects within the region.   
The Arboretum at Flagstaff ‘s native plant materials are largely used for restoration 
projects on its associated properties.  Recently, a joint project among the Coconino, Kaibab, and 
Prescott National Forests, The Arboretum at Flagstaff, and the Museum of Northern Arizona has 
worked toward creating a source of local genotype native seed for restoration projects on federal 
and private lands within northern Arizona (Kristin Haskins, Arboretum at Flagstaff, Personal 
Communication).  The project, funded by the USFS in 2007, hopes to build a sustainable native 

51 
 
plant program on the Coconino, Kaibab, and Prescott National Forests and provide baseline data 
regarding native species, their collection, and cultivation.  Because of the lack of proper 
equipment, local companies have found it difficult to harvest and process native seed.  Through 
this project needed seed production equipment has been purchased, and with the hiring of a 
program coordinator, large-scale (greater than 25 acres) native seed collections with the region 
have been completed.  To date, seeds from approximately 40 native species have been collected, 
and three seedbeds at The Arboretum have been constructed to cultivate and increase locally-
collected native species.  Seeds from the increased seed stock will be used in revegetation or 
outgrown in other facilities to increase the amounts of locally-collected seeds (Coconino 
National Forest, 2007). 
Several government agencies, such as the NPS and the USFS, contract with the NRCS 
Plant Material Center (PMC) in Los Lunas, New Mexico to research and develop native plant 
materials for use in restoration after disturbance events within the region.   NRCS PMCs have 
the ability to cooperate with public, private, commercial, and tribal partners and land managers 
to restore ecosystems using native plant material. The Los Lunas PMC provides information on 
plant material production as well as providing assistance in propagating local genotype plant 
materials for restoration projects.  By contracting with Los Lunas PMC, seeds collected from 
local sources are established in seed production fields or propagated as seedlings and 
transplanted into fields at the PMC.  These native plant materials are then sent back to restore the 
specified project area (NRCS, 2000).   However, because of costs associated with contracting 
(cleaning, inventorying, storing, propagating, and labor), development of native plant resources 
to be used on large-scale restoration projects within the region may be infeasible. Cost associated 
with contracting often increases as production difficulty increases.  For example, according to 

52 
 
contracting cost-estimate matrices developed for Montana’s NRCS Bridger PMC, the cost of 
production for small field of grass which has a low production difficulty rating is approximately 
$35.00 per pound.  The same small field of a grass which has a high production difficulty rating 
is approximately $100.00 per pound. 
As mentioned previously, NPS vegetation projects must use locally-grown native plant 
materials when available (NPS, 1996).  Native plant materials used for restoration projects in 
Grand Canyon National Park are collected and propagated within the Park, although, the 
materials produced are to be used specifically for Park projects. 
Many Conservation Districts offer a Native Plant Program which makes native seed or 
plants available to the public.  Although the Coconino Natural Resource Conservation District 
does not currently supply native plant materials, a program is being developed to establish the 
means to provide these materials (Mark Jalving, pers comm.). 
 
Current Harvesting and Propagation Techniques for Native Plant Materials 
A large amount of literature exists about native plant materials production, specifically, 
information concerning harvesting and propagation techniques of such materials. Over the course 
of many years native seed producers have begun to use available agriculture equipment for 
native seed production.  In addition, extensive research has made development of needed native 
plant materials production equipment and techniques possible. Although specific techniques 
involved in the native plant materials production process may vary, critical components include: 
seed harvesting, cleaning, storage, propagation, and production.  When done correctly seed 
purity and viability is increased and therefore more marketable. 
 

53 
 
Seed Harvesting 
Timing of seed harvest varies among native plant species and is highly dependent upon 
their origin, seed maturity rates, climate conditions, and method of harvest (Tucson PMC, 2004; 
Horton et al. 1990).  Timing of seedhead maturity tends to vary (Horton et al., 1990), however, 
on average, seed maturity takes approximately 30 days from the time of pollination (Tucson 
PMC, 2004).  During this time seeds go through several maturity stages; milk, soft dough, 
medium dough, hard dough, and mature.  For most species, seed development between the 
medium dough and the mature stage can take a few days to a couple of weeks.  Therefore, timing 
of harvest is critical (Revegetation Equipment Catalog, 26 April 2008).  Seed harvest is most 
ideal when seeds are at the medium to hard dough or mature stage.  Seeds collected too early 
(milk-soft dough stage) will experience poor germination rates, while the highest germination 
rates and storage capability occur at the hard to mature seed stage.  (Tucson PMC, 2004).  
Generally, once the first seeds begin to release the majority of the seeds are ready for harvesting 
(Stevens et al., 1996).    
With the use of commercial machinery, a variety of seed harvesting techniques make it 
possible for large-scale harvest of seeds during different stages of seed maturity.  Seed 
harvesting techniques can vary depending on seed type and size.  Direct combining allows 
harvest to be completed in one operation and is the method most often used to harvest seed from 
species with uniform maturity rates and height (Horton et al., 1990; Stevens et al., 1996).  The 
direct combining method requires seeds to be at the hard dough or mature stage.  Swathing is a 
technique in which desired fields are cut, and the crop is then placed into a windrow to further 
ripen before being combined.  Swathing allows harvesting of seed at the medium to hard dough 
stages and is most used to harvest species with shatter prone seeds (Tucson PMC, 2004; Horton 

54 
 
et al., 1990).  Swathing followed by combining is common for harvesting grass seed and species 
whose seeds mature unevenly (Horton et al. 1990; Stevens et al., 1996).  
Stripping of seed heads by hand or machine (brush harvester/strippers) is commonly used 
to harvest species with light and chaffy seeds, species with long awns or pubescence, species 
whose seeds mature unevenly, or species which are indeterminate (Revegetation Equipment 
Catalog, 26 April 2008; Scianna and Majerus, 2002; Tucson PMC 2004).  Vacuum units can also 
be used to harvest light and chaffy seeded species (Revegetation Equipment Catalog, 26 April 
2008).  Stripping collects mature seed from the plant and leaves immature seed which is 
collected later when those seeds are fully mature (Tucson PMC, 2004).  Hand stripping of seeds 
is used for wildland collections or shrubs.  Various hand-powered seed strippers or brushes are 
available for harvesting of some grass and forb species.  Seeds, berries, or fruit from most shrubs 
are collected into bags, baskets or tubs.   Use of collector hoops in combination with badminton 
or tennis rackets can also be used (Revegetation Equipment Catalog, 26 April 2008).  
 
Seed Cleaning 
While harvesting, contaminants such as weed seed, other crop seed, and material such as 
stems, leaves and immature and broken seed also get collected. These impurities can be 
separated from the desired crop seed by various seed processing machines.  Desired seed can 
then be separated further based on physical properties such as size, weight, surface texture and 
shape.   Conditioning or pre-cleaning removes excessive materials prior to cleaning, minimizes 
seed loss, and improves cleaning efficiency (Tucson PMC, 2004).  The primary machines used 
for conditioning or pre-cleaning are scalpers, debearders, hammermills and huller scarifiers 
(Tucson PMC 2004; Revegetation Equipment Catalog, 26 April 2008).   

55 
 
The most commonly used method of seed cleaning for many grasses, forbs, and shrubs is 
through the use of air-screen separators. Air-screen separators use a combination of air, gravity, 
and screens to separate seed based on their size, shape, and density.  Other seed machines which 
separate seed based on these properties include: gravity separators, velvet rolls, spiral and indent 
cylinders, indent disks, magnetic separators, and vibrator separators, along with many others 
(Revegetation Equipment Catalog, 26 April 2008).   
 
Seed Storage 
Proper storage plays a key role in maintaining seed viability.  The percent moisture at 
which to store seeds in bulk varies.  In general native seed at 10-15% moisture is considered safe 
for storage (Tucson PMC, 2004; Horton et al., 1990; Stevens et al., 1996).  Seeds kept dry and 
stored under cool low humidity conditions maintain optimal seed viability and germination 
(Tucson PMC, 2004; Horton et al., 2004).  Under these conditions seeds can stay viable for up to 
3 years in storage (Stevens et al., 1996) or longer. 
 
Propagation Techniques 
Some species can be propagated either from seed or vegetatively.  Seed propagation 
requirements depend on the species.  Some species can be directly seeded while others may 
require pre-treatment in order to break seed dormancy prior to germination.  Seed pre-treatments 
involve stratification, scarification, or a combination of both.  Stratification is the process of 
subjecting seeds to warm/moist or cool/moist temperatures.  Seeds are placed in a moist medium 
and stored under one or a combination of stratification conditions for several weeks or in some 
cases several months.  Scarification is used for species with hard seed coats and involves 

56 
 
methods which degraded the seed coat by scratching, abrasion, or soaking in acid (Native Plants 
Network, 13 March 2008).   
Many species, especially those that root easily, can be vegetatively propagated by leaf, 
stem, or root cuttings.  This method is preferred when seeds are limited, have low viability, or 
have long dormancy periods (Scianna and Majerus, 2002; Native Plant Network, 13 March 
2008).  Dormant cuttings root well after soaking for 48 hours immediately after removal from the 
parent plant (Scianna, 1996). 
Propagation protocols have been established for many native plant species which grow in 
northern Arizona.  These protocols specify ideal propagation techniques for the specified species 
and include recommendations for pre-treatments and planting techniques or requirements.  The 
Native Plants Propagation Protocol Database is a searching database of propagation protocols for 
native plants of North America (http://nativeplants.for.uidaho.edu/network/).   Many NRCS Plant 
Materials Centers have also published extensive literature on techniques and requirements for 
native plant species propagation, seeding, and planting (http://plant-materials.nrcs.usda.gov/).   
 
Seeding Techniques 
Seeding techniques can vary depending of species growth habits (grass, forb, shrub, or 
tree).  To obtain optimum seed germination and growth rates, properly selected fields should 
contain no weeds and have well-drained soils (Stevens et al., 1996).  Direct seeding consists of 
sowing harvested seeds directly into a seedbed, either in growth containers in a greenhouse or in 
fields.  Most shrubs and some forbs can be directly sown into a properly selected and prepared 
field.  For species that are not affected by transplanting shock can have seeds germinated in a 
greenhouse (Scianna and Majerus, 2002) and be planted as containerized transplants in the field 

57 
 
(Native Plants Network, 13 March 2008).  Many grasses are successfully germinated by using 
the broadcast seeding technique, in which seed is scattered over the soil surface.  When using 
this technique it is essential to ensure good seed to soil contact.  This technique is not desirable 
when the weather is hot and dry and/or the soil moisture content is low (Tucson PMC, 2004).   
Accurate seeding rates and proper contact between the soil and seed helps to increase 
seed germination and competition with weeds.  A large variety of seeding equipment (Spreaders, 
Seeders, Drills, Planters, Rollers, and Packers) is available to deliver seeds at an accurate, 
uniform rate, and even depth and provide proper soil contact (Horton et al., 1990).  It is 
recommended that seeding rates be applied for Pure Live Seed (PLS) which is the purity and 
germination of seed expressed in percent.  A seeding rate of 30 to 50 PLS is recommended, with 
larger seeds sown at up to 30 PLS per linear foot and smaller seeds sown at approximately 50 
PLS per linear foot.  Recommended seeding rates for forb species is 10-25 PLS per linear foot 
(Tucson PMC, 2004; Scianna and Majerus, 2002).    
Ideal seeding depths range from one-quarter to one inch.  Seeding depths of one-quarter 
to one-half inch are recommended for smaller seeds, for instance, perennial grasses (Tucson 
PMC, 2004; Horton et al., 1990).  Proper seeding depths for forb and shrub species range from 
one-eighth inch for small seeds and one-half inch for larger seeds (Steven’s et al., 1996). The 
range of seeding depths is often influenced by soil type/structure and seed size.  For example, 
planting of seeds in the one-quarter to one-half inch range is ideal for sowing in heavier soils 
whereas recommendations for seeds sown in lighter soils specify depths closer to one inch 
(Tucson PMC, 2004).  Most native plants will have higher seed yield in wider row spacing.  Row 
spacing highly depends on available equipment, the species, and water availability (Tucson 
PMC, 2004; Horton et al., 1990).   

58 
 
Germination requirements, which vary according to species will determine the most ideal 
time for seeding (Stevens et al. 1996).  Seeding is optimum when adequate moisture is present to 
ensure seedling establishment (Horton et al. 1990).  For the northern Arizona region this occurs 
in early spring or late summer to fall.  For forbs and shrubs, seeds that require stratification 
(moist, cool treatment) or scarification (scratching to break the seed coat), or for seeds that are 
slow to establish are best planted in the late fall.  Seeds with rapid germination rates can be 
planted in early spring, late summer, or late fall when adequate moisture is available and plants 
have time to establish before the onset of extreme summer heat or frost (Stevens et al., 1996).  
Irrigation and fertilization can help improve germination success, seedling establishment, and 
seed yield (Stevens et al., 1996; Horton et al., 1990) when weather conditions are not optimal. 
Weed control during seedling establishment is also recommended. 
In conclusion, proper native plant material production techniques have been improved, 
and the equipment needed for production exists.  Within northern Arizona, a handful of facilities 
and mechanisms are available to collect, store and/or propagate native plant materials.  However, 
the amount of land capable of agricultural development is inadequate which makes large-scale 
native plant materials production difficult.   Furthermore, seed collection and processing 
equipment are the most limited within the region.  It may be that agencies and organizations have 
not purchased this equipment because the region lacks production fields.  However, if enough 
areas were found to be usable for native plant production, a large investment would have to be 
made in obtaining the proper equipment. 
 
 
 

59 
 
Current Native Plant Cooperatives and Groups 
As a result of the 2002 report to Congress entitled Interagency Program to Supply and 
Manage Native Plant Materials for Restoration and Rehabilitation on Federal Lands, and 
initiation of the National Native Plant Materials Development Program, many interagency 
programs have been established.  These programs focus on developing an increased supply of 
native plant materials to be used for restoration practices.  The Great Basin Restoration Initiative 
(GBRI) has perhaps been largest and most successful.  Within the Colorado Plateau region 
specifically the Uncompahgre Plateau Project (UP Project), the Colorado Plateau Native Plant 
Initiative (CPNPI), and the Northern Arizona Native Seed Alliance (NANSA) have each been 
created as an interagency effort. 
 
Great Basin Restoration Initiative 
The BLM GBRI works to provide technical assistance and obtain funding to support 
increased use of native plant materials for restoration of rangelands within Great Basin region 
(BLM, 27 April 2007, Briefing Statement) (Figure 4.1).  Although the GBRI is outside the study 
region, it serves as the best available model for initiation of a native plant materials market 
within the northern Arizona region.   
In 1999, GBRI helped to initiate the Great Basin Native Plant Selection and Increase 
Project, a multi-state, collaborative research project among the BLM GBRI and the USFS, 
Rocky Mountain Research Station which focuses on the evaluation, selection, and increase of 
native seed production within the Great Basin.  Main objectives of the project include: (1) 
increasing the availability of native plant materials to restore native plant communities of the 
Great Basin, (2) developing seeding technology and equipment to re-establish native plant 

60 
 
communities, (3) transfer of information and research results pertaining to native plant materials 
to seed growers and land managers. Major research involves the development of seed transfer 
guidelines, identification of propagation techniques, and examining interactions between 
invasive and native species (USFS, 2007).  
 
                                
 
Figure 4.1: Great Basin Restoration Initiative boundary map.  Adapted from BLM website: 
http://www.blm.gov/nifc/st/en/prog/fire/more/gbri/map.html 
Uncompahgre Plateau Project 
The UP Project was initially developed to address declining ecological health and winter 
habitat for deer and elk on the Uncompahgre Plateau.  It has since become a collaborative effort 
among state and federal agencies, utility companies, and a multitude of local citizens, 
governments and organizations within upper Colorado Plateau region with the main intention to 
expand their efforts throughout the entire Colorado Plateau region.  The Uncompahgre Plateau 

61 
 
Project aims to restore natural ecological functions on the Uncompahgre Plateau by 
incorporating research and outreach on fuels reduction, invasive species control, and native plant 
seeding and restoration (BLM, 9 September 2007, Uncompahgre Plateau Project; UP Project, 
2007).   
In 2002, the UP Project developed a Native Plant Program in order to provide genetically 
appropriate native plant materials to restore native plant communities within the upper Colorado 
Plateau region.  The primary focus of the program is to develop native seed through research and 
seed increaser fields and, ultimately, to provide source identification and data on cultivation to 
commercial seed producers.  The UP Project is currently working with 44 native species, with 25 
of those species in seed increaser fields (UP Project, 2007).  Continued research involves: 
cultivation, life history, seedbed ecology, competition and spacing studies, natural recruitment, 
field application techniques, and species adaptability of native species on the upper Colorado 
Plateau (UP Project, 16 April 2008, Executive Summary).  These studies have allowed the UP 
Project to make available seed stock of 15 species to commercial growers while providing them 
with necessary information on irrigation and spacing requirements, necessary soil amendments, 
and harvesting and cleaning techniques.  The UP Project also offers a buy back program as an 
incentive for growers to start producing these new species (UP Project, 2007). 
 
Northern Arizona Native Seed Alliance 
 
The NANSA is a working group which formed in March, 2007 to facilitate the 
development and production of native plant materials for the use in restoration, rehabilitation, 
and improvement projects throughout the northern Arizona region (Haskins, 2008).  An 
important aspect of NANSA involves information sharing among its members, which are 

62 
 
comprised of individuals, government agencies, non-profit organizations and commercial 
businesses from the region.  Collaboration on native plant research is also a main objective of the 
group.  Research interests of the group include investigating requirements and techniques for 
growing native plant materials, selecting genetically and ecologically appropriate species to be 
used in revegetation projects on the Colorado Plateau, and examining the economic feasibility of 
growing native plants in the northern Arizona region (Springer, 2007).   
 
Colorado Plateau Native Plant Initiative 
 
There is an increasing demand for site-adapted native plant materials for the Colorado 
Plateau region (Figure 4.2).  In recognition of these needs, representatives from USFS, BLM, 
NANSA, Northern Arizona University, state wildlife agencies, and the UP Project are working to 
form the Colorado Plateau Native Plant Initiative (CPNPI).  The CPNPI hopes to become an 
interagency, multi-regional native plant materials program.  The goals of CPNPI are similar to 
those of GBRI and the UP Project in that it will (1) develop an adequate supply of native plant 
materials, (2) develop proper restoration methodologies, and (3) engage in the sharing of 
information with public and private entities.  Through research and outreach CPNPI aims to 
successfully support restoration of native plant communities by establishing long-term strategies 
for the production of genetically appropriate native plant materials as well as creating outreach 
materials and scientific publications on the use of native plant materials for restoration (CPNPI, 
2007). 
The formation of these groups has been critical in developing an increased amount of 
materials needed to meet current restoration needs.  Specifically, the GBRI and the UP Project 
have provided a wealth of information which has assisted in the development of subsequent 

63 
 
groups.  Their efforts can thus be used as models when initiating a native plant materials market 
within the region.  The Northern Arizona Native Seed Alliance has just recently developed and 
much work is needed to reach its goals.  However, it has already be successful in collaborating 
with multiple federal, state, private, and non-profit entities which appears to be a critical 
component in initiating further development of native plant materials.  
 
 
Figure 4.2: Colorado Plateau Native Plant Initiative boundary map.  Adapted from UP Project 
website:  http://www.upproject.org/cpnativeplant_program/cpnpi.htm 
 
 
  
 

64 
 
Survey Methods  
To assess the opportunity to initiate a native plant and seed industry within northern 
Arizona, surveys were developed for the supply and demand markets.  The overall purpose of the 
surveys was to assess current seeding practices and procedures and to identify and analyze 
current and future native plant material needs and concerns.  Through an extensive interview and 
searching process, participants were identified.   Preliminary information gained from interviews 
helped guide survey question development.  Pre-testing allowed for assessment of survey 
question comprehension, efficiency, relevancy and format organization.  From pre-testing 
feedback the final surveys were created and administered by a web-based approach.  The 
techniques used to develop and administer the survey proved to be effective and yielded high 
response rates.  The information gained highlights key elements that may be needed to stimulate 
a native plant market.   
 
Sample of Demand and Supply Survey Respondents 
A target group of potential survey participants was established for both supply and 
demand markets.  Demand survey participants included agencies and organizations within 
northern Arizona which were actively involved in restoration.  Supply survey participants 
included current production companies involved in the sale of native plant materials. 
Potential demand survey participants were identified by consulting agencies and 
organizations who were heavily involved in restoration projects within northern Arizona.  
Demand survey participants included personnel from federal, state, private and nonprofit entities 
that are currently or potentially involved in rehabilitation/restoration practices which use native 
plant materials within northern Arizona.   

65 
 
Due to the lack of native plant materials production suppliers within northern Arizona, 
potential supply survey participants included current plant material production companies 
throughout Arizona, nearby Western and Great Plains states, and those states which contained 
successful commercial seed production companies.  Native plant materials companies 
participating in the study were located in: Utah, Arizona, Colorado, New Mexico, California, 
Nebraska, Nevada, Oklahoma, Washington, Oregon, Idaho, Wisconsin, Minnesota, Montana and 
Wyoming (Table 4.1).  These plant material production companies engage in both retail and/or 
wholesale native plant material sales.  In addition, individuals, identified through preliminary 
background interviews, who showed potential interest in initiating a native plant materials 
production company within northern Arizona, also were added to the supply group sample.

66 
 
Question Development and Survey Construction 
To understand the context and design of a useful survey, a preliminary background 
investigation was conducted to determine current issues and trends pertaining to the use of native 
plant materials.  Interviews were conducted with natural resource managers from regional 
government agencies, private entities, and non-profit organizations, and private native plant 
landscaping and nursery owners within northern Arizona (Table 4.2).  The preliminary 
information was used to help guide in the development of relevant survey questions.  After the 
content of the surveys was determined, preliminary supply and demand surveys were created.   
 
Table 4.2: Summary of background interviews by organization/agency type 
Types of agencies/organizations 
Number or Interviews 
Regional Government Agencies 
5 
Private Entities 
3 
Non-profit organizations 
2 
Total 
10 
 
The research operations manager of Northern Arizona University’s Social Research 
Laboratory (SRL) was consulted for the survey’s design and content.  This guided the use of 
proper survey language and clear, unambiguous and concise wording to help eliminate question 
bias and survey fluency.  Survey questions were grouped into appropriate context categories.  In 
order to conduct the study, an Initial Application for Research along with the survey instrument 
was submitted to the Institutional Review Board at Northern Arizona University.  The 
application was approved in October 2007. 
Once question order, content, and format were finalized, a web-based survey was 
programmed for both the supply and demand markets using SurveyMonkey 

67 
 
(www.surveymonkey.com).  SurveyMonkey is an online survey tool which was selected for its 
ease of use and capability to create professional surveys.  
A web-based survey methodology was selected over traditional mail survey methods due 
to its capability for automatic verification and storage of survey responses using database 
technology as well as its ability to eliminate transcription errors (Andrews, Nonnecke, and 
Preece, 2003).  Data, exported into an Excel spreadsheet was checked to eliminate programming 
errors.  This method eliminated unanswered questions by use of special features and reduced 
administration time and cost.  Use of question “skip logic” enabled the respondent to be 
redirected to a relevant question within the survey based on the previous response.  To alleviate 
questions from being skipped entirely, all questions were programmed so the respondent could 
not continue without answering a question.   
 
Survey Content 
The demand survey consisted of 42 questions. The supply survey contained 37 questions 
(Appendix A).   The average length of time to complete the survey was approximately 15-20 
minutes.  Each survey began with an introduction page.  The purpose of an introduction page 
was to help establish a trusting relationship with the prospective respondent and encourage the 
respondent to complete the survey (Andrews, Nonnecke, and Preece, 2003).  The introduction of 
each survey included: (1) identification of the researcher, (2) explained the survey’s purpose, (3) 
explained how the results would be used, (4) guaranteed respondent confidentiality and 
anonymity and, and (5) explained the sampling methodology. 
Questions were organized according to topic relevance.  Demand survey topics included 
native plant material purchasing and expenditures, use and needs, issues and concerns, 

68 
 
equipment and operations, collaboration and funding, and policies and regulations.  Supply 
survey topics included native plant material sales, future demand, issues and concerns, 
equipment and operations, funding, and policies and regulations.  
To better understand details in these areas, both suppliers and purchasers were asked a 
series of specific questions regarding each topic.  Questions within each topic area were designed 
to gain insight on how a native plant market could be initiated within the region.  Specifically, 
expenditures or sales questions helped investigate native plant spending and sales trends.   
Questions regarding use and need were asked to gain information concerning quantities of native 
plant materials being used and the types of practices for which those materials were being used 
for.  By inquiring about future native plant materials demand, market trends could be assessed.  
Native plant material issues/concerns questions were designed to gain information concerning 
genetics, market limitations, ethical aspects of plant materials and seed costs.  Questions 
concerning equipment and operations helped to identify the available tools needed for 
development of a native plant materials market.  To help identify current and future funding 
options which would support a native plant market, questions about collaboration and funding 
were asked.  Lastly, policy and regulation questions were asked to gain information regarding 
current policy for rehabilitation/restoration work and purchasing requirements for native plant 
materials. 
 Each survey consisted of a variety of formats including close-ended, “select all that 
apply,” and open-ended questions.  Close-ended questions, where respondents selected an 
answer from a given list also included an “Other” category and, at times, a comment section.  
The “Other” category allowed the respondent to select an answer that was not on the 
predetermined list.  “Select all that apply” questions allowed for the survey participant to select 

69 
 
multiple answer choices.  Open-ended questions were included to encourage the survey 
participant to expand on issues not fully addressed in a closed-ended question format and 
enabled the responses to be assessed in a qualitative format. 
Participant memory recall is often limited when information being requested is too far in 
the past.  For this reason, questions were designed for the survey participant to recall information 
one year prior to the current year (2006).  For each question, a “Don’t Know” and “Decline to 
Answer” answer category was available to allow the respondent the choice not to answer a 
question.  Before exiting the survey, a short paragraph thanked survey respondents and an 
explanation of how the survey results would be used was explained.   
A large body of literature exists about the role incentives play, and their effect on survey 
response rates.  There is overwhelming agreement among social scientists that incentives 
increase overall response rates (Ryu et al., 2005).  For this reason, a copy of the final feasibility 
study report and a chance to win a Starbucks Coffee Company donation was offered as an 
incentive to each survey participant.  Starbucks Coffee Company generously donated $100 worth 
of Starbucks coffee and merchandise to be used as an incentive to complete the survey.  Each 
survey participant was entered into a drawing for a chance to win the prize.   
 
Survey Pre-testing 
 
A total of two pre-tests, one paper version and one web-based version were completed 
prior to the administration of the finalized web-based surveys.  Prior to the development of web-
based survey questions, draft paper-based survey questions were administered to the Northern 
Arizona Native Seed Alliance (NANSA) for feedback on survey content.  Revisions were made 
according to NANSA feedback and a web-based survey was created.  Fifteen professionals were 

70 
 
asked to complete the web-based supply and demand surveys as a pre-test.  This allowed for 
testing survey question comprehension, efficiency, relevancy and format organization.  Seven 
natural resource managers from the northern Arizona region and three NAU researchers familiar 
with native plant material practices were selected to pre-test the demand survey.  Three 
government agency personnel directly involved in purchasing or housing seed for restoration 
practices and two representatives from native plant material production companies located in 
Arizona were selected to pre-test the supply survey.  Of the 15 survey pre-test participants, three 
individuals were included in the final sample database as they were major entities in the demand 
for native plant materials.   
Prior to administering the web-based pre-test surveys, each potential survey pre-test 
recipient was contacted by telephone to explain the purpose of the survey and to gain consent to 
complete the survey.  It has been demonstrated that pre-notification contact has the strongest 
response rate impact (Kaplowitz et al., 2004).  Upon gaining approval to take the survey, the pre-
test group was sent a personalized email with instructions on how to complete the survey as well 
as the survey link.  A unique identifying number was assigned to each survey pre-test 
respondent.  This created a coded survey link which allowed survey responses to be associated 
with the respondents’ contact information.   
All survey pre-test respondents were given approximately two weeks to complete to the 
survey.  A follow-up email was sent one week into the survey response period to those 
respondents who had not yet submitted their survey.  Studies have shown that a follow-up email 
often has a positive effect on response rates (Andrews, Nonnecke, and Preece, 2003).  At the end 
of the two week survey response period, eight out of ten demand surveys were completed (80% 
response rate) and three out of five supply surveys were returned (60% response rate).  All 

71 
 
responses and feedback from the pre-test group were evaluated and surveys were amended to 
reflect relevant recommendations from this group.    
 
Survey Distribution 
The survey sample database files were finalized with a total of 42 demand and 39 supply 
contacts (Appendix B).  An attempt was made to contact each potential survey participant 
directly by telephone prior to sending the final survey link.  Pre-notification explained the 
purpose of the survey, how the results would be used, and to gain approval to participate.  Of the 
42 demand participants, 31 could be reached on the telephone and agreed to take the survey.  Of 
the 39 supply contacts, 27 could be reached directly on the telephone and agreed to participate in 
the study.  For the remaining 11demand participants and the 12 supply participants who could 
not be reached, messages were left on answering machines or with other associates.  All potential 
survey participants, including those not reached directly on the telephone, were sent their 
relevant surveys to allow for participation and increase response rates.  Survey links were 
embedded into personalized emails as well as instructions on how to successfully complete the 
survey, a deadline date, and an explanation of the Starbucks prize which was used as an incentive 
to complete the survey.  All survey participants were given approximately two weeks to respond 
to the survey.  A unique identifying number was assigned to each survey participant.  This 
identifying number allowed survey responses to be associated with the respondents’ contact 
information.  Additionally, the identifying number allowed for follow-up of ambiguous answers.   
Consequently, survey responses remained confidential and anonymous and were reported as an 
aggregate.   

72 
 
A reminder email with each coded survey link and a deadline date reminder was sent one 
week into the survey response period to those respondents who had not yet completed the survey.  
At the end of the first survey response period, 29 returned a complete demand survey (69% 
response rate) and 25 completed the supply survey (64% response rate).   
Participants who had not yet responded to the surveys after the two week response period 
were contacted again.  A total of 13 demand and 14 supply contacts were called directly by 
telephone.  The survey administration method previously used was followed again.  At the end of 
the second week response period the demand survey gained eight additional completed surveys 
and increased the response rate to 88% (37 responses total).  The supply survey gained four 
respondents, increasing the response rate to 85% (33 responses total).  Contact by telephone prior 
to sending the surveys, personalized emails, follow-up contact, and an incentive led to a high 
response rate.  Applying these tactics increase response rates, augments the validity and 
reliability of the survey data, and the resulting research conclusions. 
 
Analysis of Survey Results 
Final response datasets for each survey were downloaded from the SurveyMonkey 
website in Excel then formatted and imported into Statistical Package for the Social Sciences 
(SPSS) software (SPSS Inc, Chicago, Illinois).  The SPSS combined results for surveys in each 
dataset.  Numeric values were coded to reflect actual answer choices.  Survey questions were 
analyzed by calculating answer frequencies (n) and valid percents of respondent participation for 
each question.  For respondents answering “Don’t Know” and “Decline to Answer,” those 
responses were counted as missing and were not included in the valid percent calculations.  For 
multiple choice questions stating “Select all that apply,” total percent may exceed 100, as 

73 
 
respondents could answer multiple times.  Valid percents, in these cases, were calculated by 
dividing the total response for each category by the total number of responses for that question.  
Percents were rounded which may cause total percents to be slightly greater or less than 100. 
Answers within a “comment” or “other” response were tabulated and analyzed.  
Responses fitting into the original question categories were recoded accordingly and included in 
final valid percent and frequency results.  For ease of the analysis, open-ended and other 
responses were summarized.  For question where open and other responses that were repetitive 
are listed below the corresponding question and delineated with the number of duplicate 
responses in parenthesis.  Questions and corresponding results are discussed in the survey results 
section and summarized in an Annotated Questionnaire format (Appendix C). 
 
Demand Survey Results  
Native plant material purchasing and expenditures 
 
When respondents were asked to provide the amount of seed (in pounds) purchased for 
seeding practices in the last year, over half of respondents answered equally between 0-100 
pounds and 101-500 pounds of seed purchased (26% each).   
Overall, when respondents were asked to specify the amount of native seed purchased (in 
pounds) and in the last year related to ecological restoration, burn area rehabilitation, roadside 
mitigation, flood abatement, wildlife habitat improvement, and range rehabilitation most 
respondents purchased native seed for burn area rehabilitation and ecological restoration 
activities (Figure 4.3).  Almost two-thirds (61%) of all the native seed purchased (in pounds) 
during 2006 was for burn area rehabilitation activities.   
 
 

74 
 
 
Figure 4.3: Seed purchased (in pounds) in 2006 for ecological restoration and burn area 
rehabilitation 
 
For ecological restoration, over half (54%) of respondents answered either 0-100 pounds 
(27%) or 101-500 pounds (27%) of native seed was purchased.  An equal amount of respondents 
(30% each) said between 101-500 pounds and 1,001-5,000 pounds was purchased for burn area 
rehabilitation.  For roadside mitigation, a little over two-thirds (67%) said they purchased 0-100 
pounds, and 50% said they purchased 101-500 pounds for wildlife habitat improvement.  Buyers 
of native seed did not purchase seed for flood abatement or range rehabilitation practices (Figure 
4.4).   
In the next five years considering that agency/organization programs grew at an optimal 
rate, almost one-quarter (23%) of respondents stated that 0-100 pounds of native seed would be 
necessary for their programs needs.  “Other” responses noted that the amount of native seed 
needed was highly dependent upon future fire size and fire frequency. 
When respondents were asked about the amount of money spent on native seed in 2006, a 
little over one-quarter (26%) of respondents stated their agency/organization spent between

Native plant material use and needs 
A series of questions were asked to assess whether or not regional organizations/agencies 
currently implemented seeding and if so how much native plant materials were being used in 
relation to specified land management practices.  In addition, these questions aimed to identify 

79 
 
factors affecting whether to purchase of native plant materials and future needs of specific 
species to be used in restoration.  
The majority of respondents (82%) currently implemented seeding.  Of those respondents 
currently involved within seeding, a little over one-quarter (26%) said they primarily implement 
seeding for ecological restoration.  Wildlife habitat improvement and burn area rehabilitation 
came in a close second for the purpose of implementing seeding (22% each).  Respondents 
answering with “Other” said they implemented seeding after timber operations, construction 
activities, and for cattle forage (Figure 4.10).

Native plant material issues/concerns 
A single question was asked to determine land managers’ perception of the term “local 
genotype.”   Follow-up questions aimed to discover the most salient issues related native plant 
materials use and limiting factors to obtaining seed locally.   
Although over one-quarter (29%) of respondents defined local genotype as a native plant 
coming from a local source (within a 50 mile radius), some respondents answering “Other” 
expressed that northern Arizona’s extreme elevation and climatic conditions make it difficult to 

83 
 
specify an exact definition of local genotype and that the definition is also highly dependent on 
the species in question. 
An overwhelming majority (93%) of respondents said that their organization or agency 
was concerned about the genetic source of native seed.  Furthermore, most respondents (59%) 
choose not to use non-native plant materials for seeding practices.   However, respondents agreed 
that the primary limiting factor keeping their organization/agency from buying seed locally was 
the lack of availability (50%).   
The majority of respondents (87%) foresaw a need for local genotype native plant 
materials for seeding practices within the next five years.  Moreover, respondents revealed that 
availability of seed (27%) and cost of seed (22%) were the greatest obstacles to overcome in 
order to initiate a successful native plant materials market within northern Arizona (Figure 4.14).

Native plant equipment and operations 
 
To assess availability and need for native plant materials production equipment and 
facilities, respondents were first asked if they collected native seed for seeding projects. There 
was no notable difference in the number of respondents stating that they do collect (53%) and 
those stating that they do not collect native seed (47%).  Of the organizations/agencies that 
collected native seed, one-third (33%) collected less than ten pounds within the last year while 
28% collected 10-50 pounds.   
When respondents were asked what mechanisms or facilities they have to collect, store, 
and/or propagate native plant seed, over one-quarter (27%) said that they had no such 
mechanisms or facilities to process native seed while 18% said they have storage facilities. 
 
Collaboration and funding 
Collaboration occurred most often between federal (22%) and state agencies (20%). Most 
respondents stated they would like to work with federal and state agencies (17% each) in the 
future on native plant projects. Over one-half (57%) of respondents said they currently receive 
federal or state monies or incentives to assist with seeding practices with a little over half (52%) 
receiving funding from federal dollars, excluding federal incentive programs.  
When respondents were asked what programs were available to their organization/agency 
that encourage increases in local native seed production, over one-quarter (28%) of respondents 
stated that  restoration programs were the most available to their organization/agency.  
Respondent answers varied widely when asked to list three native plant programs (first to third 
choice) that would be the most useful to their organization or agency.  Most responses shared a 

85 
 
common theme that revealed any program leading to an adequate production of local native seed 
would be important. 
 
Native plant policy and regulation 
 
Of the federal, state, private, tribal and nonprofit entities to which this survey was 
administered, most respondents fell within the “federal” or “state” categories (47% and 27% 
respectively).  One-quarter (25%) of respondents said that organization/agency policy was the 
main deciding factor in purchasing native seeds while a little less than one-quarter (21%) thought 
availability of native seed was also important.  Project acreage appeared to the most important 
deciding factor when determining how much native seed is purchased. 
The majority (80%) of respondents said their organization or agency currently required 
the use of certified noxious weed-free native seed.  For those respondents who did not currently 
require the use of certified seed, 67% said they anticipate requirements to use certified seed 
within the next five years.   
Most respondents (80%) were not willing to enter into a five year contract with a certified 
native seed company.  Open-ended comments to this question disclosed a variety of factors that 
need to be considered.  Additionally, most respondents (74%) were not interested in becoming a 
supplier of native seed.  This is logical since respondents for this survey are buyers of native 
seed. 
 
Supply Survey Results  
Native plant material sales  
Most supply survey respondents (70%) stated they were currently involved in a business 
of selling native plant materials.  Respondents not currently involved in selling native plant 

86 
 
materials indicated they were a government agency, and policy does not allow them to sell native 
plant materials, or they had no interest.  
Of the types of native plant materials sold (trees, shrubs, grasses, forbs, or seeds), almost 
one-third (32%) of respondents stated that their business was involved with the sale of native 
seed (Figure 4.15).

The majority of respondents (97%) were currently in the business of selling native seed.

accrued by selling strictly native seed.

(Agropyron spp.) were the most commonly sold.  Respondent answers varied widely in species

would predict to be the highest in demand for large-scale seeding projects within the next five

appeared to be in highest demand (91%).  Bluebunch grass (Pseudoroegneria spicata) and Indian 
ricegrass (Achnatherum hymenoides) had the highest answer frequency among all first through 
fifth choice responses (18% of the total responses).  Among all the responses (n=53), the five 
most frequently stated species for seeds thought to be in highest demand, in order of popularity 
included: bluebunch wheatgrass, indian ricegrass, western wheatgrass (Pascopyrum smithii), 
thickspike wheatgrass (Elymus lanceolatus), squirreltail (Figure 4.16).

Considering the first choice responses for grasses, many respondents identified various

Native plant material issues/concerns 
Although almost one-third (32%) stated local genotype can be defined as native plant 
materials coming from a local source (within a 50 mile radius), respondents answering “Other” 
expressed that a definition of local genotype is highly dependent upon topography of a specific 
region, climatic conditions, habitat, and the species being considered.  
In subsequent questions, respondents identified that producing and/or using local 
genotype native plant materials was “somewhat” (47%) to “very important” (33%).  Further 
comments revealed that producing local genotype plant materials is difficult due to the costs and 
the limited resources that are available during the collection process.  In addition, suppliers are 

89 
 
more interested in producing what is currently in demand.  Despite the limited production of 
local genotype native plant materials, the majority of respondents (70%) agreed that there is a 
current market for selling local genotype native plant material for large-scale restoration projects. 
Most respondents stated that lack of a consistent and reliable demand (38%) and lack of 
knowledge about native plant material production (21%) were the most significant limitations to 
a business involved in the production of native plant materials (Figure 4.17).

When specifically asked about the percentage of production cost spent on labor for the entire

6-10% and 21-30% of their production costs on labor.   
 
Funding 
Most respondents (62%) stated current native plant operations were supported by internal 
funding, while one-third (33%) were supported by both internal and external funding.  Of those 
stating that the majority of their native plant operations were supported by either internal or 
external funding (36% and 41% respectively), most respondents (41%) said internal funds were 
the main sources of funding.

Native plant policy and regulation 
When respondents were asked if their seeds were certified noxious weed-free seeds, 
almost half (46%) stated some were.  The top two reasons for seed certification are state 
regulations and/or ecological ethics (27% each). 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 

93 
 
V. Discussion  
This was an exploratory study, and native plant materials market surveys were conducted 
to assist in providing baseline information needed to further evaluate native plant market 
opportunities in northern Arizona.  The information gained will help guide subsequent research 
questions and development of future studies.  However, there were several limitations to the 
surveys.   Most notably is the small sample size (demand survey n=42, supply survey n=39).  A 
sample size of 42 and 39 renders a margin of error of 15.4% (1/√42) and 16.0% (1/√39) 
respectively.  Most social researchers look to have a margin of error of ≤ 5%, which means a 
sample group of ± 500 would be needed to yield information in which more accurate 
assumptions could be made about an entire population (Niles, 2006).  Furthermore, the sampling 
methodology used consisted of two types of non-probability sampling techniques: (1) purposive 
sampling (sampling specific groups or types of people based on the purpose of the study) and (2) 
snowball sampling (existing study subjects recruit future subjects from among their 
acquaintances) (Babbie, 2004).  Both of these sampling methods do not allow for an equal 
chance of all buyers and suppliers in northern Arizona to be selected.  Therefore, this 
methodology does not lend itself to statistical analysis and the ability to generalize the findings 
to a larger population.  Therefore, conclusions should be considered cautiously. Second, due to 
the lack of suppliers within the region of study, surveys were administered to suppliers outside 
the region.  These businesses often supply native plant materials to buyers within ecosystems 
very different from northern Arizona.  Information obtained from outside suppliers was at times 
not relevant to the issue the question was designed to answer.  For example, questions designed 
to identify the most important species to have brought into production provided an abundance of 
species which are not used within the region and assumptions regarding needed native plant 

94 
 
species could not be made.  Moreover, sizes of the seed companies surveyed varied and answers 
given showed a clear distinction between producer sizes and, at times, made overall assumptions 
difficult to make.  Therefore, conducting the survey among similarly sized seed companies 
would help to obtain more congruent data.  Thirdly, question design is responsible for some un-
interpretable data.  At times, answers disclosed that questions were unclear and may have been 
misunderstood.  Several respondents also indicated that because of the complexity of the several 
questions, time and or memory recall made it difficult for them to give precise answers.  This 
may be improved by simplifying questions.  For example, a question within the demand survey 
asked respondents to list their top ten choices of seed.  Questions like these are time consuming 
and may deter respondents from continuing with the survey.  Pre-testing is a good way of testing 
survey question comprehension, efficiency and completing multiple pre-testing sessions could 
help produce more concise questions.  Lastly, choice of the survey instrument delivery mode was 
an important factor in gaining the highest quality of data possible.  Although web-based survey 
instrument has many positive impacts on response rates and analysis of the information gained, 
at times it made clarification of questions impossible and therefore a telephone survey may 
improve the quality of information gained by providing immediate clarity of questions and more 
control of answer responses.   
Despite these limitations, these surveys make several important contributions to the 
study.  First, the surveys successfully identified several important factors to consider when 
initiating a native plant materials market in northern Arizona.  Second, vital insight on the 
disconnect between both the demand and supply markets was provided.  With the information 
and insight gained, useful recommendations on how to proceed with initiation of the market can 
be made.  Third, the need to further investigate native plant market opportunities in the northern 

95 
 
Arizona region is apparent.  Subsequent research will benefit from the exploratory nature of this 
project and the baseline information obtained with survey limitations clearly identified. 
The demand and supply surveys identified many limiting factors to consider when 
initiating a successful native plant materials market in northern Arizona.  From a demand 
perspective, lack of availability and cost has kept purchases of native seed minimal even though 
there has been increased policy favoring their use.  Coupled with this, it appears that within the 
current market there is a variable demand for native plant materials due to unpredictable fire size 
and frequency and policy decisions continued use of non-native plant materials.   On the supply 
side, an inconsistent and unreliable demand has made suppliers hesitant in further development 
of native plant materials for this particular market.  In addition, an abundance of information is 
available for successful native plant production, however; this information is not adequately 
disseminated and, subsequently, many suppliers still lack the required knowledge to successfully 
produce native plant materials.   Many of these factors need to be overcome for successful 
initiation of a localized native plant materials market.    
The majority of native seed purchased (in pounds) and money spent on native seed in 
2006 was for ecological restoration and burn area rehabilitation.  Overall, in 2006, burn area 
rehabilitation programs purchased more seed and allocated the most funds then any other 
restoration/rehabilitation activities.  The BLM, which manages more land (264 million surface 
acres) (Davis, 2001) than any other federal agency, spent more on native plant materials in 2006 
than any other agency surveyed (USFS, NPS, ADOT, NRCS, and AZGFD).    
Burn area rehabilitation seems to play a large role in the demand for native plant 
materials. Each year, millions of dollars are spent on post-fire emergency rehabilitation 
treatments (Robichaud et al., 2000) for the main purpose of erosion control and watershed 

96 
 
protection.  Seeding is the most common treatment used for post-fire rehabilitation and soil 
stabilization (Beyers, 2004) and, although in recent years the trend has been towards an increased 
use of native species, the lack and cost of these materials have allowed for the continues use of 
non-native species.  Over the last 30 years, specifically within Arizona and New Mexico, the 
number of acres burned by wildfire in the Southwest and the amount of money allocated for 
post-fire seeding in those areas has increased dramatically (Figure 5.1).
