97 
 
Within fire-prone ecosystems where understory vegetation carry frequent low intensity 
ground fires, most native species are adapted to reproducing rapidly after a fire event (Keeley et 
al., 2006).   However, research applying predictions from Global Circulation Models (GCMs) 
consistently suggests that with climate change the western United States will continue to 
experience increases in fire frequency and severity as a direct result of increased temperatures 
and drought conditions (Nitschke and Innes, 2006; Flannigan et al., 2005).   Increases in fire 
frequency and severity have shown to be important in controlling post-fire ecosystem structure 
and function.   A changing fire regime may cause an overall loss of nutrients in the ecosystem 
resulting in increased plant mortality and invasion of non-native species (Turner and Romme, 
1994; Thonicke et al., 2004; Schoennagel et al., 2004; Wang and Kemball, 2005; Freeman et al., 
2007).  These changes will ultimately affect the ability for post-fire native plant community 
reestablishment.  Therefore, the potential for changes in fire frequency and severity underscores 
the importance of protecting native plant communities from predicted changes in their abundance 
and function.    
Research have shown that pre-wildfire vegetation treatments, such as tree thinning, 
prescribed burning, mechanical fuel treatments, native plant seeding, and other treatments could 
be used to reduce negative effects of ecological changes on native plant communities by 
increasing native species structure and function, diversity, and biomass productivity (Menke, 
1992; Brockway and Lewis, 1997; Covington et al. 2001).  Nonetheless, with an increase in fire 
frequency and severity, the need for emergency post-fire rehabilitation efforts specifically that of 
post-fire seeding will persist.  Thus, the future market of native plant materials may be driven by 
an increased demand for post-fire seeding materials.  However, care should be taken to ensure 
seed used for post-fire seeding is not contaminated with non-native species since these species 

98 
 
have the ability to further disrupt essential ecosystem processes.   More importantly, the use of 
local genotype plant material will be valuable in returning proper ecosystem function to post-fire 
environments and increasing their ability to be more resiliant and adaptable to a changing 
environment (Huenneke, 1991).   
Based on the results of both the demand and supply surveys, consensus in both groups 
revealed that defining “local genotype” is difficult because of elevation, climatic conditions and 
consideration of the species in question, although, both sides tend to agree that defining the term 
“local genotype” is seed from a local source (within a 50 mile radius).   Both seed companies and 
land managers conveyed they are concerned with the genetic source of seed.   However, 
according to survey results, the majority of the seed supply purchased in 2006 came from out-of-
state sources but within a defined ecological region containing geographically distinct 
assemblages of natural communities and species (e.g. Great Basin, Colorado Plateau, etc.), and 
although most organizations/agencies stated they prefer not seeding or planting non-native plant 
materials, a rather high percentage said they still use non-native plant materials.  The lack of an 
adequate supply of local genotype materials and high costs associated with those materials 
appear to have a substantial influence on decisions whether to purchase native plant materials 
and where those materials are obtained from.   
Policy often drives decisions on whether to purchase native seeds.  Currently, most 
agencies have policies requiring the use of native seeds, although, lack of native seed availability 
has annulled these policies, and use of non-native species continues.  Most organizations / 
agencies surveyed require the use of certified seed or anticipate doing so in the near future.  
Today, a large amount of the seeds used in revegetation activities are certified (Kujawski and 
Ogle, 2005).    The process of seed certification is intended to verify source of the seed, help 

99 
 
preserve genetic purity and identity, and decrease the likelihood that seed mixes on the market 
are contaminated with noxious weed species.    Additionally, the Arizona Crop Improvement 
Association mandates that all certified Variety/Cultivar seeds be tested to verify seed 
germination and purity.  Therefore, certified seeds are usually of higher quality and purity and 
have greater germination potential (Dunne, 2002).  Many federal and state revegetation policies 
now incorporate the use of genetically appropriate native plant materials, and new alternative 
Pre-Variety Germplasm seed certification methods have become essential to the procurement, 
production, and distribution of these materials (AOSCA, Native Plant Connection Bulletin).  Pre-
Variety Germplasm seed certification acts to assure genetic integrity of native seeds on the 
market, and is an important tool used to preserve native plant populations and ensure success to 
revegetation projects.    
 According to survey results, due to current policy shifts requiring the use of certified 
native plant materials most commercial native seed producers are able to provide certified seed 
and have begun to do so because of state regulations or their own ecological ethics.  The survey 
did not specifically inquire about whether native seed producers are able to charge a premium for 
the certified materials they provide.  However, due to extra cost involved in the certification 
process, prices for certified native plant materials often reflect the company’s investment to do 
so.   
The native seed industry has begun to play a larger role in producing genetically 
appropriate materials.  However, limitations associated with producing those materials makes 
increased production difficult.  Many suppliers stressed that producing these materials is not 
always practical because of the limited ecosystem ranges these materials are adapted to, cost of 
collection, and low probability of selling those materials.  Despite these limiting variables, the 

100 
 
majority (70%) of suppliers believe there is a current market to sell local genotype plant material.  
However, demand for these materials dictates their availability and most suppliers are more 
interested in producing and selling native plant materials which are currently highest in demand.   
Most companies surveyed were outside the northern Arizona region; therefore species in demand 
within their region may be well outside the northern Arizona region.   
A future need for a supply of local genotype plant materials is evident and, in order to 
initiate a successful native plant materials market within the region, constraints of availability 
and cost of native seed need to be overcome.   Without locally-adapted native plant materials, 
high prices will persist and purchasing of materials from non-local sources will continue.  An 
increase in demand for local genotype materials may stimulate local market opportunities and 
therefore an increase in the supply of genetically preferred species.  Costs for local genotype 
plant materials may be lowered if an abundant supply is produced and if increased demand is 
perceived.  Moreover, producers and organizations/agencies should come to a consensus on the 
scale “local genotype” is defined.  Therefore, an increase of locally-adapted plant materials can 
be developed accordingly.   
Both surveys helped to identify the most important species each side would like to have 
brought into production.  Most suppliers and buyers predicted that seeds of grass will be in 
highest demand in the next five years.  Species of native grass have been on the market 
regionally outside of Arizona for some time.  It has been noted that grass species are the highest 
in demand because of their broad range of adaptability (Steve Monsen, Retired USDA Forest 
Service, pers comm.).  Furthermore, the majority of native plant production research has gone 
into the development of grass species.  From this, an abundance of knowledge and equipment for 

101 
 
their development exists.  However, survey results indicated inconsistency when trying to 
identify exactly which species of grass will be needed.   
The species of grass, forbs, and trees/shrubs identified as highest in demand for buyers, in 
order of frequency selection were blue grama, penstemon and globemallow, and fourwing 
saltbush.  Additionally, land managers conveyed interest in purchasing ecosystem specific seed 
mixes.  Meanwhile, in comparing the native plant species suppliers predict as highest in demand 
results were somewhat misleading.  As stated previously, suppliers surveyed were outside of 
northern Arizona and, due to the ecosystem differences; their market may have different 
demands.    For example, suppliers predicted various species of wheatgrass as highest in demand, 
although some species of wheatgrass, in particular crested wheatgrass (Agropyron cristatum) and 
intermediate wheatgrass (Thinopyrum intermedium), are considered to be non-native perennial 
species (Richards et al., 1998; Springer et al., 2001; Beyers, 2004) within northern Arizona.  
These species would be considered undesirable for restoration projects within the region because 
of their ability to outcompete locally-adapted species for resources leading to decreased native 
plant propagation and diversity (Blair, 2001).  Species considered for forbs and trees/shrubs were 
a closer match on the supply and demand sides.  In order of frequency selection, blue flax, 
globemallow, and yarrow were selected for forbs, while sagebrush and fourwing saltbush were 
identified for tree/shrub species in highest future demand.   The supply/demand link with forbs 
and trees/shrubs may be due to these species tendency to a broader native range as compared to 
grasses causing demand for these species to be similar across a broader range of markets. 
Buyers within regions where commercial seed companies are prevalent increase the 
demand for species which are desired in their region. Therefore, the supply of non-local and 
sometimes undesirable species will continue to increase.  Many of the desirable grass species 

102 
 
appear to already be available through commercial seed producers outside the region.  Therefore, 
producing a large enough supply of these species locally and providing them at a competitive 
rate may be difficult.   This pressure could be alleviated by focusing on producing native species 
which are needed but that lack a multi-regional demand.  
The native seed industry is highly competitive, and variable seed prices are driven by 
imbalances between supply and demand and cost associated with species production.  The 
average price for native seed is most often higher than non-native seed.  Native species pose 
extra processing difficulties due seed shattering and seed dormancy issues.  For those species 
which are perhaps difficult to produce or for which demand is low the price per pound of seed is 
much higher than for those which are produced in large quantities.  For example of the 13 
species buyers identified as the top seed, grasses, forbs, trees/shrubs (Appendix C) the cost per 
pound of muttongrass is exceptionally high, whereas the cost of blue grama fairly low (Table 
5.1).  The high cost for muttongrass may reflect difficulties of production or low demand.   
Alternative Pre-Variety Germplasm certification methods help to facilitate procurement, 
production, and distribution of native plant materials and increase the quality of native seed and 
should lead to an increase in demand.  As demand increases, more seed is produced, which 
enables the cost per pound of seed to steadily decrease.   
Resources available for seed production also aid in determining current market prices.  In 
terms of production, supply survey results showed agreement that seed storage facilities, land, 
seed cleaning machines, seed collection tools and labor are necessary in order to supply a reliable 
amount of native plant materials to meet the current demand within the region.  The 
machines/facilities recommended for each step of native plant production process varied but, all 
are readily available through various agriculture equipment companies (Table 5.2) 

103

Furthermore, supply survey results revealed that the number of acres identified as most 
often used for native plant materials production was over 100 acres, although field collection of 
seed plays an important role in the production of native plant materials and this most often takes 
place in wildland settings.  Wildland collecting could reduce the number of acres needed for seed 
production and is the most feasible option when obtaining specific species which would be too 
costly to produce in production fields.  However, seeds collected in this fashion are mostly 

104 
 
gathered by hand-picking and the overall supply from wildland sources may be much less than 
the demand.

The survey revealed that collection of native seed within organizations/agencies occurs, 
however, many organizations/agencies lack proper equipment or facilities to collect and process 
seed efficiently so their efforts have yielded relatively small outputs.  Furthermore, many 
organizations/agencies involved in small-scale seed collection often depend on voluntary labor 

105 
 
with collection efforts led by people who have limited experience in seed collection.  As a result, 
the amount of viable seeds obtained is minimal.  Several agencies are able to take advantage of 
NRCS Plant Materials Centers (PMCs) abilities to process seed, labor, space, and time is often 
minimal.  However, based on the amount of seed needed, seed collection and/or NRCS PMCs 
may not be feasible and quantities produced may not be sufficient for restoration/rehabilitation 
activities within the region.   
A handful of facilities and tools are available and used to collect, store and/or propagate 
native plant materials within the northern Arizona region which are spread between federal, 
state, and non-profit entities (Table 5.3).  However, although an abundance of seed production 
equipment exists for purchase, seed collection equipment and seed processing equipment are 
limited among all agencies and purchasing these tools would be beneficial in order to begin 
developing a more adequate supply of locally-adapted native plant materials.   
Costs associated with native plant materials production and land needed for production 
appears to be high.  In 2007, the Uncompahgre Plateau Project (UP Project) investigated the cost 
and time associated with development of native plant species for release as cultivars.  Based on  
annual operating costs for the UP Project and the Great Basin Restoration Initiative (GBRI), the 
estimated cost per species for collection, research, increase and release is approximately 
$15,000-25,000 per year and the average timeframe for the development of a species ranges from 
4-12 years (CPNPI, 2007).   An interview with Granite Seed Company revealed that more than 
10,000 acres is needed to supply large-scale restoration projects with the materials needed 
annually (Don Bermant, Granite Seed Company, Personal Communication).  The survey 
revealed that most commercial seed companies expend the most money on seed collection and 
labor.

The survey also showed that the majority of commercial seed companies in the West are 
private growers who receive no external funding.   Private growers often have no restrictions on 
who is able to purchase the native plant materials produced, but obtaining proper funding may be 
difficult for private growers.  Alternatively, state and federal entities often receive funding and 
have equipment available, but, policies on the sale of seeds prohibit seed to be sold for profit.    

107 
 
There are a limited number of native seed suppliers within northern Arizona.  Thus, 
identifying potential suppliers is critical for market initiation.  From the demand survey results, 
five local entities expressed an interest in becoming a supplier of native seed.  It should be noted 
that survey respondent input was not obtained from individuals outside the targeted sample 
group (n=42).  Thus, the number of individuals interested in becoming a supplier of native seed 
is not exhaustive and may be higher than what the survey results indicate.   Nevertheless, two-
fifths of the respondents currently have seed storage capabilities while only one-fifth stated that 
greenhouses, seed collection machines, and irrigation were available.  All five potential local 
suppliers indicated the lack of funding, time, and labor are major obstacles in supplying native 
seed.   Without the proper amount of skilled labor, sufficient funding, and necessary equipment, 
obtaining and processing large quantities of native seed is not feasible.   Partnerships between 
private growers and government agencies may prove to be beneficial in improving funding for 
native plant production and equipment sources.  For example, the GBRI and the UP Project has 
created successful partnerships with private growers.  These partnerships have enabled GBRI 
and the UP Project to develop an increased amount of native plant materials needed to meet 
current demands. 
As a part of the BLM’s GBRI the Great Basin Native Plant Selection and Increase Project 
(GBNPSIP) has worked with private growers over the course of many years to help increase 
native plant materials.  GBNPSIP acquires funding through an Interagency Agreement between 
the USDA Forest Services’ Rocky Mountain Research Station, Shrub Sciences Laboratory in 
Provo, Utah and the BLM.  Total funding for the project was approximately between $4.5 
million to $6 million from 2001 to 2006 (GBRI, 200; Pellant, 2006) with their first year of 
funding totaling $800,000.  The Uncompahgre Plateau Projects’ Native Plant Program, which 

108 
 
was modeled after GBRI, has received direct and in-kind funding from the BLM, USFS, 
Colorado Division of Wildlife (CDOW), Utah Division of Wildlife Resources, NRCS, National 
Fish and Wildlife Foundation (NFWF), Western SARE, and the Colorado Mule Deer 
Foundation.  From 2002 through December 2007 the program has received approximately 
$2,437,000 million in funding, with the majority of total funding coming from the BLM (50%) 
and the USFS (35%).  During the initial stages of UP Project funding equaled approximately 
$500,000 per year.  However, funding has decreased every year and the Project now operates on 
about $350,000 per year.  This amount pays for one year-round full time employee, four full time 
seasonal, two lease pickup trucks, gas, equipment, overhead for UP staff, and cooperating 
facilities (Pam Motley, UP Project, Personal Communication).  According to the UP Project 
Fiscal Year 2007 Progress Report, total expenditures for the program from 2002 to December 
2008 totaled approximately $2,290,000.  It appears that a substantial amount of federal funding 
would be needed to initiate a similar native plant materials project in northern Arizona (Table 
5.4).

Lack of a consistent and reliable demand is a major obstacle to those who supply native 
seed.  Many demand survey respondents indicated that the amount of seed (in pounds) needed 
within the next five years was low (0-100 pounds).   Most seed that is purchased goes in 

109 
 
rehabilitating burned lands and the low estimates of the amount of seed needed could be a direct 
result of fire size and frequency as difficult factors to predict.  When investigating further, the 
“other” responses revealed that this amount is highly dependent on fire size and frequency and 
affects project acreage, another key determinant when deciding how much native seed was 
purchased.   
Unpredictability of fire size and severity ultimately leads to an inconsistent and 
unpredictable demand, another limiting factor leading to insecurity in the market.  Many 
commercial seed producers feel limited by the lack of consistent and reliable demand for native 
plant materials as well as their lack of knowledge about production techniques which are needed 
to successfully develop native plant materials.  Increased information sharing between producers, 
agencies, and scientists could help to increase development of reliable materials.  Furthermore, 
from a supply perspective the risk is far too high to invest time and effort into producing 
materials which may not be sold or yield large enough quantities to be profitable.  Expansion of a 
native plants market into the Colorado Plateau region may make those materials produced more 
easily sold by catering to a larger group of buyers.   Developing ways to guarantee a consistent 
demand would also encourage suppliers to initiate native plant production.  Long-term contracts 
with federal agencies have been used as tools to create market guarantees.  However, when 
organizations/agencies were asked if they would be willing to enter long-term contracts with a 
certified native seed company, the majority (80%) stated “no,” with “other” comments revealing 
that current agency policies do not allow long-term contracts.   This option may need to be 
explored further.   
Some organizations/agencies have established restoration programs and, although they 
have helped to increase research and development of native plant materials, amounts of native 

110 
 
plant supplies are still limited.  In order to initiate a successful native plant market additional 
programs leading to a more adequate supply of locally-adapted native plant materials would be 
useful.  These programs would thus act to increase the research needed for further native plant 
materials development.  By collaborating with federal and state agencies, funding for 
restoration/rehabilitation may be possible. 
 
 

111 
 
VI. Conclusion and Recommendations 
 
Increased severity of disturbance events, revegetation policies, and concerns about the 
genetic consequences of using non-local plant materials have led to a high demand for locally-
adapted plant materials to support the increasing numbers of large-scale revegetation projects 
within northern Arizona.  However, many factors have become constraints hindering the 
development of a native plants market.  Before northern Arizona can move forward in initiating a 
market, effort should be concentrated on resolving several issues this study has identified 
including the need for:  (1) a clear definition of the term “local-genotype” agreed upon by all 
entities involved in the native plant market, (2) consistent management decisions across agency 
boundaries concerning the use of native plant materials, (3) increased availability and lowered 
cost of native plant materials, (4) a consistent and reliable demand for native plant materials, (5) 
increased communication and information sharing among producers, land managers, buyers, and 
researchers (6) stronger collaboration/partnerships among federal, state, private, and non-profit 
entities, and (7) a stable funding mechanism for the development of native plant materials. 
Both suppliers and purchasers agree that locally-adapted materials are important but, the 
extreme elevation and climate conditions within northern Arizona region make it difficult to 
come to agreement on an exact definition of the term “local-genotype.”  Defining this term will 
be an important factor in determining the composition of the local supply and which species are 
produced.  A promising finding from the survey revealed grass seed appears to be in highest 
demand within the region.  Focusing on grass seed development may be important during the 
market initiation phase. 
Because of increased policies on certification and the use of native plant materials, most 
commercial seed producers are able to provide native and certified seed, but, these materials 

112 
 
often come at a higher cost.  The overall importance of using certified seed is a clear indication 
that the native plant market and native plant communities would benefit from a standard policy 
making the use of certified seed mandatory on all revegetation projects regardless of agency type 
or size.  Stronger policies on the use of certified seed would help increase demand for these 
materials, thus, eventually decreasing their cost. Furthermore, due to recent research efforts, 
agencies have begun to recognize the importance of using locally-adapted plant materials, and 
federal and state revegetation policies now incorporate the use of genetically appropriate native 
plant materials for restoration.  However, management decisions lack consistency across agency 
boundaries and, it appears that cost and availability of these materials continues to limit their use, 
making implementation of stronger native plant policies difficult.   
The lack of availability and high cost of native plant materials prevent buyers from 
purchasing these materials. Many organizations/agencies have begun to supply some of their 
own native plant materials.  However, seed collectors often lack the level of skill needed to 
correctly identify and collect viable seed.  Although some of these groups have obtained 
equipment for production, the equipment being used lacks the necessary quality needed to 
produce native plant materials on a large-scale basis.  Producing an adequate supply of locally-
adapted plant materials which are sold at a reasonable cost will be needed in creating a localized 
market.   
It appears that providing a higher more consistent demand will be essential to lower 
market prices.  Suppliers are hesitant to produce native plant materials because of the lack of 
knowledge associated with producing those materials and the inconsistent and unreliable 
demand.   Growers are reluctant to grow native plant species if successful seed production, 
harvesting, and processing information is not available.  Information and technology transfer 

113 
 
would encourage potential suppliers to grow species needed and improve the quality and cost of 
the materials produced.  Similarly, buyers are more likely to purchase materials of high quality 
and viability which are vital factors that lead to a successful restoration project.  Therefore, 
increased research developing important production protocols for species identified as most 
important to the northern Arizona native plant materials market will be important before getting 
reliable seeds into the hands of producers.   
Stewardship contracting and buy-back programs have been successfully initiated through 
multi-agency agreements have been used to provide suppliers the guarantee of demand that need.  
For example, in 2004 the Apache-Sitgreaves National Forest offered a 10-year stewardship 
contract to thin 150,000 acres of ponderosa pine within the forest considered to be vulnerable to 
fire and insect attacks.  Through this contract, the development of wood fiber industries was 
encouraged by providing a guaranteed supply of wood to those industries for ten years (Neary 
and Zieroth, 2007).  Both the GBRI and the UP Project have created buy-back programs to 
encourage commitment from growers and accelerate development of native seed supplies.  Seeds 
developed by the Great Basin Native Plant Selection and Increase Project or collected from BLM 
areas of concern and are provided to growers.  In turn, contracts are made for agencies to buy-
back a portion of the seed quantities produced (GBNPSIP, 2002).   Creation of a stable demand 
should be a top priority.  Long-term contracts or buy-back options to growers could provide a 
mechanism in which a consistent demand can be given to growers.   
Over time, a large amount of information concerning native plant materials production 
has been acquired.   The GBRI and UP Project are good examples of groups which have acted to 
stimulate markets in neighboring regions and act as an important source of production and 
market information.  The GBRI and UP Project can be used as models and, although their region 

114 
 
of interest encompasses landscapes different than northern Arizona, their efforts have provided 
examples which could help initiate a native plant materials market.  The success of the GBRI and 
UP Projects can be attributed to several important components.  Both have resulted from 
collaborative efforts among federal and state agencies, private entities and local organizations, 
and the need for improving severely degraded rangeland conditions.  The GBRI has been 
supported by regionally integrating federal and state funding appropriations (GBRI, 2001), while 
the UP Project receives the majority of its funding through partnerships with federal, state, 
private, and nonprofit entities, as well as independent sources (UP Project, 2007).   
Northern Arizona encompasses some of the United States’ most diverse ecosystems, and 
the effects of large-scale disturbances could be mitigated by restoring native plant communities.  
The region is also home to a multitude of federal, state, private, and nonprofit entities.  Multi-
agency collaboration/partnership could create needed sources of funding to increase locally-
adapted revegetation materials.  Successes achieved by GBRI and the UP Project may also be 
attributed to their regions’ long history of agriculture and favorable climate conditions.  
Therefore, the extreme environmental conditions within northern Arizona cannot be overlooked 
and may present the most difficult variable to overcome when initiating a market.  First, water is 
scarce and drought conditions are predicted to continue and become more severe.  Second, a 
great deal of uniform land is needed to develop large quantities of native plant materials, 
specifically grasses.  Extreme elevation and precipitation gradients are also present within the 
region.  Elevation and precipitation are important factors in determining vegetation communities.  
These gradients change abruptly and, therefore, vegetation communities can change within a 
short distance.  Thus, uniformity within the landscape is rare.  Many areas within the region also 
contain soils which may not optimal for native plant production purposes.   

115 
 
With an increasing demand for locally-adapted native plant materials within the region 
and an interest in its development, a market opportunity exists.  Northern Arizona clearly poses 
unique challenges to native plant production and a diverse approach to initiating a market may be 
needed.  However, it is clear that a coordinated effort is needed to increase communication 
between producers, land managers, buyers, and researchers.   Poor communication within 
existing native plant markets has hindered their progress.  Increasing communication would 
assist in market development and increase the possibility for market success.   Communication 
should aim to establish agreements on a common set of definitions and standards for native plant 
materials, the types and amounts of materials needed to be produced, costs associated with native 
plant materials, and areas of priority for future research.  A large effort should also focus on 
accumulating, organizing and disseminating any available information on native plant markets 
and production.    
Although in its infancy, the Northern Arizona Native Seed Alliance (NANSA) has begun 
to address many of these issues through coordinating and information sharing among producers, 
land managers, buyers, and researchers.  It aims to facilitate research investigating the 
requirements and techniques needed for growing native plant materials and selecting genetically 
and ecologically appropriate species to be used in revegetation projects in northern Arizona.      
Further investigation of issues this study identifies will help to determine the most 
feasible approach to initiating a native plant market within the region and solutions to the most 
salient issues this study has identified.   Specifically, further investigation of options that would 
secure funding through collaboration for market initiation would be useful.  Additionally, 
stewardship contracting and buy-back options should be explored further to help ensure suppliers 
a guaranteed demand.   This study has identified principal species buyers would like to see 

116 
 
brought into production.   An economic study evaluating costs associated with development and 
production of each species along with species profitability margins would be beneficial.   
Identifying individuals interested in producing native plant materials with land capable of 
production would be helpful.   Furthermore, the information gathered from the survey could be 
used to create a useful database of Arizona native seed suppliers and buyers of native seed.  This 
database could be available to current suppliers and buyers as well as the public online and/or 
could be printed in a directory form.  Creating and administering a more advanced survey where 
the questions are improved and sample size is increased would be beneficial to future research. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 

117 
 
Study Summary 
 
1. Changing climate conditions, fire suppression, non-native species invasion, exploitation 
of rangelands, urbanization, and use of nonlocal genotype plant materials has lead to an 
alteration of soil and plant resources and an overall loss of native plant community 
productivity and diversity as well as disrupting the ecosystems they rely on (Milton et al., 
1994; Monsen and McArthur, 1995; Holmgren and Scheffer, 2001).   As disturbance 
events increase, revegetation of disturbed lands will be needed, and the use of locally-
adapted plant materials will become increasingly valuable to restore genetic diversity.  
Thus, demand for locally-adapted native plant materials will increase. 
2. Many revegetation policies currently require the use of native species “when available”, 
but because of the limited quantities produced, agencies continue to use non-native 
species for revegetation of disturbed wildlands.  Although policies have made available 
funding to develop various native plant programs to increase the supply of native plant 
materials, these programs have been unable to produce these materials on a large-scale 
basis.  Agencies have begun to recognize the value of using locally-adapted plant 
materials when vegetating disturbed lands and new policies on their use have arisen. 
However, management decisions on their use lack consistency across agency boundaries 
while high costs and lack of availability of these materials makes implementation of 
stronger native plant policies difficult.   
3. The amount of large-scale restoration projects has increased due to an increase in 
disturbance events throughout northern Arizona.  Federal, state, private, and non-profit 
organizations alike are experiencing the need for locally-adapted native plant materials to 
restore these disturbed areas.  Currently, there is a lack of abundance of these materials 

118 
 
within the region.  Research on native plant materials production has increased and the 
information and equipment needed for production have become available.  The GBRI and 
the UP Project have acted as models to help create regional efforts and provided the 
information needed to assess the initiation of a native plant market within the region. 
4. Based on the GBRI and UP Project as models, the estimated cost per species for 
collection, research, increase and release is about $15,000-$25,000 per year and the 
average timeframe for development of a species ranges from 4-12 years.  Most of the 
funding for current projects comes from federal agencies (BLM and USFS).  The amount 
of funding needed for initiation of a similar project in northern Arizona is estimated to be 
between $500,000 and $800,000 for the first year and between $2,437,000 and 
$6,000,000 over a five year period.  Most monies should be provided by federal entities.   
5. It appears that within the current market there is a variable demand for native plant 
materials due to unpredictable fire size and frequency and policy decisions.   An 
inconsistent and unreliable demand has made suppliers hesitant in further development of 
native plant materials.  Stewardship contracting and buy-back programs could be used to 
provide suppliers the guarantee of demand they need.   
6. Lack of communication among producers, land managers, buyers, and researchers 
appears to be a major limiting factor to the initiation of a successful native plant materials 
market in northern Arizona.  A large effort is needed to accumulate, organize, and 
disseminate available information on native plant materials production.  Partnerships 
between producers, government agencies, land managers and organizations proves to be 
an effective method obtaining important native plant material information and the 
funding needed for market initiation.

Appendix C: 
 
Annotated Questionnaires 
(Demand and Supply Surveys) 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 

170 
 
Appendix C. Annotated Questionnaire - Demand 
 
A total of 42 questions were administered to 37 consumers of native seed in the Northern 
Arizona Native Plant Materials Market Feasibility Survey.  Answer frequencies (n) and valid 
percents of survey results for each question are summarized in an Annotated Questionnaire 
below. 
 
In cases where respondents answered “Don’t Know” and “Decline to Answer,” these responses 
were counted as “missing” and were omitted in calculating valid percent. In addition, valid 
percents were rounded which may cause total percents to equal slightly more or less than 100. 
For the multiple response questions, where respondents were able to select more than one 
answer, the total n may be higher than the number of respondents. In these cases, valid percents 
were calculated by the number (n) in each response category divided by the total number of 
responses for the question.  Where respondent answers were identical in the open response 
questions/sections, the total (n) duplicated responses are represented in parenthesis after the 
stated response.  See Appendix I for a full list of scientific names and authorities used in this 
questionnaire.

Q40. Are there any general comments that you would like to add regarding the topics of this 
survey? 
 
1. We are an interstate natural gas transmission company which adheres to federal, state and 
local regulations pertaining to reseeding and restoration 
2. We do not use large quantities of seed like those amounts necessary for large seedling 
project.  We produce plants/seedlings for research, wildlife habitat improvement, and fire 
rehabilitation/restoration projects.  A lot of our propagation is also from cuttings.  We 
have produced over 250,000 plants which have been put into the field over the last 3 
years.  Almost 50,000 Fremont cottonwoods and native willows in the last two years 
strictly from cuttings.  Besides seed local gardens which hold different genotypes of 
Fremont cottonwood and willows would be useful as a source for cuttings.  Local gardens 
with different local genotypes of aspen would also be useful for cuttings for future 
project. 
3. Seed pound/cost only an estimate 
4. Before the market is developed, there needs to be a concerted effort to determine the 
feasibility of planting the various native seeds through existing equipment as well as what 
the seeding and germination requirements of the species are. For example there seems to 
be very little information on how deep Needle and Thread grass seeds need  to be seeded 
not to mention how breaking the awns off the seed (as is currently done0 during 
processing affects it's ability to germinate. Not to mention if it can be effectively seeded 
in combination with other seed with different requirements. Other unanswered questions 
are how to effectively seed into established mediterranean annual stands. Until we can 
understand some of these issues, we will waste most of our time and money trying. 
5. I am sure your results will underscore the NEED for more native plant material 
production.  How will you use the results??? 
6. Our new seed collector is new and untested. We are not sure how the whole process will 
work from harvesting to processing to storage.  So, this is a big unknown factor on how 
much seed we will need from out-side sources. 
7. My answers are only relevant to Hart Prairie Preserve, not the entire Nature Conservancy. 
8. Our work is mainly on the lower Colorado River; however the seed companies that we 
obtain seed from are in San Diego and Utah. It would be great to have a local grower. 
Some seed is hard to propagate in field conditions and the plants are better planted as 
plugs. 
9. NRCS has a plant materials program that is developing native plant species for use in the 
region.  We would like to see these better utilized by growers for use in their adapted 
ranges. 
10. Development of seed sources of regional ecotypes of native species is critical for use 
post-fire, for revegetation, for weed and erosion control and for general revegetation 
needs.  Thanks for working on this project. 
11. We will utilize local native seed whenever possible.  If we had a reliable source, and 
costs, quality, supply, etc were conducive to our procurement regulations, we would 
utilize local sources as a priority. 
12. Most of our activities have been in southern Arizona or along the Central Arizona Project 
alignment through Sonoran desert biome 
13. Is there a separation between small scale and large scale markets? 

197 
 
14. Because the topic of this survey is so complicated, having a comment section associated 
each question would be helpful.  That way you could explain "yes" or "no" if needed. 
15. There needs to be a local outlet to be available to us here in Northern Arizona, possible in 
Flagstaff. 
16. I cannot speak for my agency as a whole.  I can only answer questions that relate to my 
direct experience including as few fire rehabilitation projects (BAER). I cannot address 
the willingness of the Forest Service to enter into long-term contracts 
17. We did have a seedling program for a number of years. It was disbanded and I am not 
aware of any plans for us to reinstate any of these programs. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 

198 
 
Appendix  C. Annotated Questionnaire - Supply 
 
A total of 37 questions were administered to 33 consumers of native seed in the Northern 
Arizona Native Plant Materials Market Feasibility Survey.  Answer frequencies (n) and valid 
percents of survey results for each question are summarized in an Annotated Questionnaire 
below. 
 
In cases where respondents answered “Don’t Know” and “Decline to Answer,” these responses 
were counted as “missing” and were omitted in calculating valid percent. In addition, valid 
percents were rounded which may cause total percents to equal slightly more or less than 100. 
For the multiple response questions, where respondents were able to select more than one 
answer, the total n may be higher than the number of respondents. In these cases, valid percents 
were calculated by the number (n) in each response category divided by the total number of 
responses for the question.  Where respondent answers where duplicated in the open response 
questions/sections, the total (n) duplicated responses is represented in parenthesis after the stated 
response.  See Appendix I for a full list of scientific names and authorities used in this 
questionnaire.

Other responses s Q1: 
1. It depends on the species.  For some it would be a watershed or a specific ridge, for others it 
    might be a part of several states. 
2. It depends, some things change drastically in 50 miles in Utah.  A 50 mile radius would be a 
    good start although  on occasion out of state is possible. 
3. Within similar habitat, but it depends on species 
4. Same species growing in similar climate, (rainfall, temperature ranges, soil types) can be at 
    any distance. 
5. A community of species covering similar topography, elevation. 
6. Collection of material is within a 5th order HUC (Hydrologic Unit Code, as defined by US 
    Geological Survey), used within  a third order HUC; mid-range elevational bands of 500' 
 
 

199 
 
7. 300 miles south & west -150 miles north & east- Maximum elevation increase 2000 ft.-  
    elevation  
    decrease 1000 ft. 
8. 200 mile radius                                                                                                                                                       
 
 
Q2. How important is producing and/or using local genotype plants and seeds to your 
organization/agency?  
 
 
Valid Percent 
Frequency (n) 
Very Important 
33  % 
10 
Somewhat Important 
47 % 
14 
Somewhat Unimportant 
17 % 
5 
Very Unimportant  
3 % 
1 
Total 
100 % 
30 
 
Comments Q2: 
1. Problems using local genotype plants or seeds that we run into is that they don't fit a large 
    enough area and you can run into collectors charging huge prices if the demand is great.   
    There is never enough of the types that you are looking for to seed large areas and it takes a 
    long time to grow enough to be of use.  We run into this problem all the time.   The best  
    approach is to have the plant materials centers select viable varieties that fit larger areas.  A lot 
    of the times the local genotype varieties have a difficult time become established and 
    competing. 
2. The variability of fire makes difficult to inventory quantities of local genotypes that may or  
    may not be sold. 
3. The majority of our seed sales not influenced by local genotype 
4. It is not always practical.  There is a chicken and egg problem with localized material. 
5. Very important for woody species (as required by federal law); very important for forbs 
6. The answer to this question can vary dramatically from species to species. In other words, 
    some species are more adaptable than others. 
7. It is important to us if it is important to our customers 
8. It is dependent upon customer requests - we sell a lot of native grass seeds for reclamation and 
    maintenance.

Comments Q3:

2. The problem is that we can't get good seed stock to grow enough seed to fit any project of any 
    size quick enough.   Example is if you have a project that required a collected variety.  The 
    cost at the time of bid was $100.00 per pls lb.  By the time the project was seeded the price 
    had gone up to $1,000.00 per Pure Live Seed pound because of the weather and availability of 
    the seed and the person who had tied up all the seed and raised the price.   If it is not available  
    in the commercial market place by many players the price of the seed will be huge 
3. A very small market

    locally adapted species diminishes.

Other responses Q12: 
1. You should have “All of the Above”, if you are talking about local genotype or all native 
    seeds.  It makes a great deal of difference.  Local genotype is a huge amount of time and labor 
    finding varieties that you can grow. It is extremely expensive and time consuming. 
2. Weed control 
3. For the past few years it would be available land for production.  Currently, the price of wheat 
    and corn, have impacted the available acres for production of native species. 
4. Reliable seed production of native seed species. Private increase of native seed species is 
    unreliable and varies too dramatically to make it financially worth-while. Native collection of 
    locally adapted species is more reliable and cost effective.

Q35. Are there any general comments that you would like to add regarding the topics of this 
survey? 
 
1. Going through your questions, I can't think of all the problems that we have run into with 
    regard to native seed.  You talk about 25 acre areas, what about when you have wildfires, 
    floods, or other major events.  How do you provide the genotype for the area to cover these  
    events, and if you plant seed from outside the area that you are talking about don't you 
    contaminate what you are trying to accomplish.  There is no way a present where you can 
    grow enough genotype seed to fit all the needs out there. As each region wants more and more 
    species only the cost is going to put all out of reach for any project.   I would have to see 
    genotype and other native species of the same type planted in test plots side by side in every 
    area that shows planting genotypes grown only within a small local area are better.   And I 
    haven't seen it done yet 
2. For me, it seems geared for larger operators, not small timers like myself doing this mostly for 
    xeriscaping of small properties. 
3. Location of each person answering the survey will make a big difference on the kind of native  
    business they do.  For example land managers in low precip zones are happy when anything, 
    native or not, grows.  Where a land manager in a high precip Zone >14 inches has the luxury 
    of being selective as there are far more species to choose from in that location. 
4. It is a little difficult to decide what the top sellers will be since it depends on what area you are 
    talking about. Restoration projects tend to be very species specific and that changes for each 
    location. Our success has come from trying to educate the public and we deal more with 
    individuals than agencies. 
5. Species supply and demand are highly volatile and change monthly 
6. Market dynamics/volatility defy rational decision making. 
7. I would recommend you review the Bureau of Land Management (BLM) purchases over the 
    previous 5 years.  Using that information you could get an excellent idea of which species are 
    in demand.  Visit www.fedbizops.gov for that info or contact the BLM out of Boise, Idaho. 
 
 
 

218 
 
8. Many of the questions did not apply to my small business. Though a high percentage of my 
    seeds are from native plants, my customers are wholesale nursery growers for the landscape & 
    retail nursery trades. I've worked to encourage the use of true native material, rather than 
    merely drought tolerant exotics, in the urban landscape. 
9. The most difficult aspect of supplying locally adapted species for our business is the red tape 
    and long delays related to the governing land agencies and the ability of the seed purchaser to 
    acquire funds. For example, the long drawn out process of the federal agencies procuring 
    money often outlasts the plants growing season and the plants often blossom, produce seed 
    and then shed their production before the agencies can get funding in place. This often delays 
    the agencies ability to purchase seed until the following season and the seed needs to go on the 
    ground now. The fact that federal agencies work on a fiscal year system that does not coincide  
    with the growing/harvest season does not help, either. Another problem is the following  
    example: the Fish and Game needs seed to plant a recent fire. The closest locally adapted seed 
    stand is located just over the next ridge from the planting site but is growing on US Forest  
    Service (USFS) land and the USFS won’t let you collect seed in that area. 
10. We are a small non-profit foundation and we do not do large scale projects. 
11. As I stated in my answers, the selection of the seeds and plants that will be in demand are 
      very customer and site specific. We feel that there is not one grass or plant that works well in 
      all locations and situations. Another thing to take into consideration is the constant  
      development of new viable types of native plants that are being researched and will be 
      available in the next 5-10 years. 
12. Most of the questions on this survey do not fit our company.  We distribute wildflower seeds, 
      some of which are native types -- we do not offer specific eco-types, seed grown in certain  
      areas, do native collection.  Seed native to the United States is not just grown in the United 
      States.  Your survey is more suited to companies that grow in their location for application in     
      their region or are involved in hand collection. 
13. If there is a market then you can damn well bet there will be someone there to produce what  
      is needed.
